==PAGE:7==
Abstract
This report describes the process and results of the Master Thesis, Con­
cept Development for Life Improvement of Type 1 Diabetics, written in 
collaboration with The Danish Diabetes Association. An exploration of 
the technology and context of persons with type 1 diabetes (T1D) has 
been conducted to analyse the needs, values and problematic situations 
of persons with T1D on a holistic level. 
Research has been conducted through desktop research and interviews 
with persons with T1D and diabetes professionals. An analysis of the fin­
dings revealed common themes and problematic situations. Thus it was 
found that there is a need among persons with T1D to increase predic­
tability in daily life while taking individuality and the variety of physiolo­
gical states into account. 
Persons with T1D were continuously involved during concept synthesis, 
detailing and justification. Furthermore, a creative and systematic design 
engineering approach was taken. Nine concepts were synthesized and 
presented in a concept catalogue. By the use of various evaluation met­
hods, one was chosen for detailing and justification.
The final concept is an app that aids the user in living an active life with 
T1D by ensuring that they have the relevant knowledge, inspiration, and 
a practical approach for obtaining an overview and individual insights to 
how their body and blood glucose react to exercise considering various 
influences.  
Continuous usability testing and a final user validation showed that the 
concept fulfills the need of the users by creating a better structure for 
obtaining insights on blood glucose levels in relation to exercise. Thus, 
it is recommended that The Danish Diabetes Association implements 
the concept in a scale feasible to the compentencies of the organization. 
Furthermore, this implementation should be used to create proof of con­
cept and thereby incentive for a full development of the concept.

==PAGE:9==
Reading Guide
The persons with diabetes who have been in­
volved in the project through various intervie­
ws are referred to as participants. When not 
referring to an interview, people within the tar­
get group are called potential users as these 
are the ones, we hope to make a solution for. 
When referring to use of a specific product, the 
person with diabetes is referred to as the user. 
The abbreviation T1D is used for Type 1 Dia­
betes. Everyone who has been involved in re­
lation to their profession are grouped as other 
stakeholders. 
When it is written that a person is ”low” or 
”high” it respectively refers to having a low 
blood glucose level or a high blood glucose 
level.
The authors of this report are referred to as the 
design team.
The report is supported by appendices which 
document the design team’s empiri and in­
sights handed over to the Danish Diabetes As­
sociation and by worksheets where observati­
ons are processed.

==PAGE:14==
This thesis is written in collaboration with The 
Danish Diabetes Association with the objective 
to develop a concept which improves the life of 
persons with type 1 diabetes. The thesis takes 
a value driven design approach to wicked pro­
blems and the target group, 18-40 year-olds, is 
involved in the process throughout the project.
Type 1 diabetes (T1D for short) is a chronic di­
sease where the body stops producing insu­
lin - an important hormone necessary to get 
glucose from the blood into the cells. Persons 
with T1D will need to be treated with insulin 
for the rest of their life. The disease becomes 
a big part of their daily, as the blood glucose 
level must constantly be monitored and ad­
justed with insulin, diet, and physical activity. 
The Danish Diabetes Association, the collabo­
rators of the project, is a large patient associa­
tion with approximately 90,000 members (dia­
betes.dk). They offer guidance, courses, and 
1 Introduction
a website with knowledge and information on 
diabetes for persons with diabetes and their 
relatives. They also connect pesons with diabe­
tes in network groups, local associations, and 
Facebook groups and offer products for diabe­
tes such as a free app and information start kits. 
Furthermore, The Danish Diabetes Association 
works politically to get diabetes on the agenda.
The introductory chapter first presents the 
motivation for the project. It then goes on 
to describe the overall approaches: Thin­
king principles, placement of the project 
in a lifecycle model and design and pro­
blem-solving 
process 
description. 
Final­
ly, the phases of the project are described. 
The methods presented in the introduction 
are not elaborated on as they will be explained 
under their respective phase of the project.
14
==PAGE:15==
(Andreasen, M et al., 2015).  In the continuous fra­
ming of the problem, a ‘how’ is connected to the 
outcome (Figure 3). This framing is created through 
the constant expansion of scope from idea to con­
cept to product design as seen in conceptualization 
(Andreasen, M et al., 2015).
1.2.2 Socio-Technical Thinking
Clegg, C. W. (2000) describes sociotechni­
cal principles for design which are categori­
zed as meta-, content- and process principles:
•	 Meta-principles include that design is socially 
shaped and an extended social process. In this 
thesis, social challenges connected to having 
T1D are researched, and design concepts are 
proposed to fix them.
•	 Content-principles include that “systems should 
be simple in design and make problems visible.” 
In this thesis, it is not the goal to make the most 
impressive and complex solution. The solution 
has to be as simple as possible while fulfilling 
the set task.
•	 A Process-principle is that design includes mul­
tidisciplinary education and is in itself a socio­
technical system. In this thesis, both anthropo­
logy- and engineering disciplines are used, and 
lue for the end-user.”, who are persons with T1D. 
Furthermore, in this approach “contextual com­
plex problems, with diverse stakeholders, are re­
framed to create better value for people within the 
particular context.” (Andreasen, M et al., 2015).
1.2.1 Design Abduction
The basic ‘equation’ (Figure 1) for problem 
solving is knowing the ‘what’ (elements), the 
‘how’ (pattern of relationship) and the out­
come. This was illustrated by Dorst, K. (2015).
Regular abduction is problem-solving based on 
experience. Here a how (pattern of relationships) 
and an outcome is known but not the ‘what’.  Con­
trary to regular abduction, only an outcome is de­
fined in design abduction, e.g,. life improvement 
of type 1 diabetics (Figure 2). In this project, the 
outcome is shaped through the cognitive and crea­
tive process of conceptualization “which generates 
ideas, understands value and leads to decisions”

1.1 Motivation
The Danish Diabetes Association works to improve 
the quality of life for people with diabetes. In additi­
on, they want to take part in creating projects which 
can benefit diabetics and create value to these.
The design team’s interests also lie within value 
driven design. The complex area of T1D, further­
more, presents the opportunity to work with a 
socio-technical engineering system, as it inclu­
des various types and levels of stakeholders with 
different perspectives and discourses as well as 
specific artefacts and man-machine interactions.
User-centered design is of specific interest, and it 
is important for the design team to create concepts 
which help users and improve their daily life. A 
large problem space with many actors and techno­
logies also offers the design team the oppurtunity 
to take a holistic view. Thereby, the needs of pe­
ople with type 1 diabetes considering the routines, 
artefacts, and interactions taking place in their da­
ily life can be thoroughly explored and analyzed.
1.2 Thinking Principles and 
World View
The thinking principles in this project are design 
abduction, socio-technical thinking, and systems 
thinking. The world view (the ‘glasses’ the design 
team wears), is a user-centered- and holistic view.
Kleinsmann et al. (2012) identified six approa­
ches to design based on interviews with 55 desig­
ners. This project follows the approach of design 
as value driven which “...focuses on creating va­
Figure 1: Equation for problem solving
Figure 2: Regular abduction (top) and design abduction 
(bottom)
Figure 3: Framing in design abduction
15
==PAGE:16==
the solution can be found across disciplines 
(software, marketing, industrial design etc.) 
Conceptualization is a way to structure the mul­
tidisciplinary nature of design.
1.2.3 Systems Thinking 
Züst, R., & Troxler, P. (2006) describe systems 
thinking as a special way to see reality to under­
stand complex situations. Systems thinking emp­
hasizes a process-oriented view and, similar to so­
cio-technical thinking, it is interdisciplinary. They 
also argue that it “is a desired contrast to local, 
isolated and department-related thinking.” This 
is in line with Eliott et al. (2007) who mention 
‘thinking holistic’ as a principle for systems thin­
king, which also becomes important in this thesis 
as the design team must consider the system in 
its whole including the complexity of the engine­
ering system that is the Danish healthcare system. 
1.2.3.1 Holistic World View 
Züst, R., & Troxler, P. (2006) discuss how to have 
a holistic view which they describe as ensuring to 
take the entire system into account. It consists of 
three heuristics:
1.	 Considering time-related changes: There should 
be reflections on the past, present, and future. 
This means that the translations in the devel­
opment arena have to be considered. It must be 
considered which area The Danish Diabetes As­
sociation has worked within and how the design 
and strategy connected to the concept differs 
from previous products. 
2.	 Thinking in alternatives and options:  Different 
alternatives, options and scenarios should be 
considered. Thinking in alternatives and opti­
ons is the basis of conceptualization and seen 
from the initial idea loop to the final concept. 
Scenario creation is a key support for the task 
formulation to show that the task is accomplis­
hed in a use scenario with the final concept. 
3.	 Working from a general to detailed perspective: 
Details should be increased step by step.  The 
approach of the research and analysis in this 
project is generally bottom-up as detailed per­
spectives are explored through interviews and 
worked into a more general view with the use of 
an affinity diagram. However, it is recognized 
that the design team will have rough knowledge 
about a lot of perspectives after the first phase, 
exploration. Details should then be increased

1.3 Design and Problem­
solving Process
The process is described in Figure 5 using The Triple 
Diamond (adapted from the Double Diamond, UK 
Design Council, 2015) and elements of the Encap­
sulation Design Model (Andreasen, M. M., Hansen, 
et al., 2015). The Exploration phase is described by 
the first diamond and the Concept synthesis phase 
by the second. The third phase, Detailing & Justifi­
cation, is linked to decisions for product synthesis, 
product development, and product life synthesis. 
The first phase is dedicated to gain insights and em­
pathy (Kolko, J., 2015). The following is dedicated 
to thinking in solution space and the final is dedica­
ted to executing the selected concept.
 
17
step by step. In the process of this project, the 
third heuristic is closely connected to the devel­
oping phases starting at concept synthesis, whe­
re a rough concept is created and then detailed. 
1.2.4 User-centered Design
The design team has an overall user-centered de­
sign approach in the project where the focus is on 
the potential users (persons with T1D) and their 
needs throughout the entire process. The appro­
ach also borders participatory design by applying 
co-creation methods and conducting contextual 
inquiries. With this, the approach moves towards 
user as a partner rather than user as subject as seen 
in Figure 4 (Sanders & Stappers, 2008).

1.4 Structuring of Phases
The three phases were planned using Gantt charts 
(Appendix 1) and structured as described in the 
following.
1.4.1 Exploration
First phase of the project was primarily informati­
on-problem-driven - see the four strategic dimensi­
ons for attacking a problem can be seen in Figure 
6 (Hansen C. T. & Andreasen M. M., 2008).  The 
problem space was discovered through desktop re­
search and fieldwork. Relevant actors, especially 
people with T1D, were involved through intervie­
==PAGE:18==
18
ws and observations. Furthermore, an informati­
on-solution-driven approach was taken to map out 
existing technical solutions and model the activity 
domain for these.
Based on the conducted research, an analysis and 
mapping of user needs, problems, and perception of 
ideals was conducted. 
For the analysis, a development arena was drawn to 
present the actors populating the context and field 
of development and to describe the associated de­
sign paradoxes. The users were described as per­
sonas and their needs described in a need analysis. 
Problematic situations and common themes was 
revealed using an affinity diagram and described in 
scenarios.
Based on the findings of the exploration phase, the 
design task and goal was formulated supported by a 
system demarcation. 
Throughout the Exploration the idea loop was vi­
sited, and a collection of ideas was gathered in an 
idea catalogue.
1.4.2 Concept Synthesis
The second phase, Concept Synthesis, delved into 
the solution space and concepts were created through 
creative and systematic processes. The ideation was 
kickstarted by a co-creation workshop with persons 
with T1D and the results from this were summari­
zed in another idea catalogue. Using the two idea 
catalogues, ideas were combined and developed 
applying elements of the activity domain, organ 
domain, and use scenarios connected to the ideas. 
This synthesis led to concept proposals, presented 
in a concept catalogue with a concept drawing and 
a description of the ideas in and ideas with the con­
cepts. These were evaluated based on strengths and 
weaknesses, a quantification using Weighted Objec­
tives Method, and feedback from stakeholders. The 
Concept Synthesis phase was concluded by choo­
sing one concept based on the evaluation.
This phase was primarily knowledge-solution-dri­
ven by using knowledge from the first phase and 
by utilizing the knowledge of users through the 
co-creation workshop. Solutions were also sought 
out through an information-solution-driven appro­
ach by considering existing solutions for combina­
tion of elements.
1.4.3 Detailing and Justification
In the final phase, the concept was detailed by 
making variations, detailed design of parts, and a 
final concept sketch. The concept were presented 
using low- and high- fidelity prototypes to potenti­
al users. Usability testing was conducted to shape 
the detailing process and to make a final validation. 
The impact of the final concept was evaluated with 
regards to the three engineering levels (artefact-, 
complex system-, and engineering system level). 
Strategies for implementation to market was pre­
sented using a product idea model, a strategy can­
vas, and a business model canvas and formulated 
in relation to the development arena.
1.5 Life Cycle Model for the 
Project
The project is in the development phase of a te­
chnical system’s life cycle (Züst, R. & Troxler, P., 
2006). The design team worked in the fuzzy front 
end where there is not a design proposal from the 
beginning. When initiating the project, the know­
ledge about the system is at its lowest and the influ­
ence on the system’s characteristics are high as no 
decisions are taken (Figure 7, adapted from Züst, 
R. & Troxler, P., 2006). When reaching the concept 
synthesis phase, the knowledge about the system 
had increased while the influence on the system’s 
characteristics had decreased and decreased even 
more as decisions were taken in this phase. This 
illustrates how important decisions are inherently 
taken early in the project despite uncertainty and 
incomplete information.
Development
50 %
100 %

Concept 
Synthesis

Influence on system

The Exploration phase is essential for devel­
oping the correct product as it supplies the 
data, information, and knowledge needed for 
development. Needs must be discovered and 
the final product has to be based on accurate 
and thorough inputs from the context and te­
chnology domain. The final product proposal 
also must make sense across the whole life cy­
cle and provide the best conditions for the in­
volved actors in all life cycle phases. 
In Exploration, there is a loop between discove­
ring and defining a problem formulation, need 
interpretation, perception of ideals, task per­
ception and exploring relevant technology and 
idea elements (Figure 8).

2.1 Methods and Theory
A method toolbox of contextual design, frame crea­
tion, conceptualization methods and need- and mo­
tivation analysis was used in the exploration phase.
2.1.1 Desk Research on Diabetes and 
Technology
Research on T1D and other types of diabetes were 
conducted, focussing on the basics of the disease as 
well as similarities and differences between T1D 
and other types. Furthermore, different devices for 
insulin treatment and blood glucose monitoring 
were mapped. A more detailed overview and under­
standing was established by considering the activity 
domain (Andreasen, M. M. et al, 2015) of some of 
the most common devices:  Product life activities, 
operation activities and use activities were model­
led. 
The desk research was used as preparation for user 
involvement to ensure a fundamental understanding 
of the disease and the artefacts which the users meet.
2.1.2 Contextual Design
Contextual Design starts with one-on-one intervie­
ws with participatns in the task setting (Holtzblatt, 
K., & Beyer, H., 1997). Contextual inquiries reve­
al hidden structures and issues which would be less 
likely to be seen in a traditional interview setting. 
Furthermore, when there is both vision and speech 
for communication instead of only one, the design 
team’s understanding and empathy in the issue be­
comes enlarged. The data simply becomes more de­
tailed and concrete. “If designers start from abstra­
ctions, not real experience, and then abstract again 
to go across all customers, there is little chance 
the system will actually be useful to real people.” 
(Holtzblatt, K., & Beyer, H., 1997).
Contextual inquiries are in their nature either 
non-structured or semi-structured as the questi­
ons are partly based on observations (Kvale, S., & 
Brinkmann, S., 2009). It was chosen to make them 
semi-structured to keep a focus, and therefore, an 
Interview guide was used for this purpose.
User involvement is crucial for project success. A 
study from Standish Group from 1995 found that 
the number one factor for project success was user 
involvement and the number two factor for project 
failure was lack of user involvement. In 2015, user 
involvement was the number two factor for project 
success (Appendix 2).
2.1.2.1 Work Models 
Originally the work models were made to document 
work processes at a workplace. In the case of this 
project, they were used to document tasks and routi­
nes in the daily life of having diabetes. It is com­
parable to a work setting as the persons with T1D 
are bound to manage extra planning because of their 
diabetes and have to operate artefacts to be their 
’own doctor’. These were therefore considered to be 
the ’work’ of persons with T1D.
There are five kinds of work models which cover dif­
ferent perspectives of the work system (Holtzblatt, 
K., & Beyer, H., 1997):
•	 Sequence model: Shows the detailed work steps 
necessary to achieve an intent.
•	 Artefact model: Shows the physical things crea­
ted to support the work, along with their structu­
re, usage, and intent.
•	 Cultural model: Represents constraints on the 
work caused by policy, culture, or values.
•	 Flow model: Representing the communication 
and coordination necessary to do the work.
•	 Physical model: Shows the physical structure of 
the work environment. The physical model is not 
used in this case as the work of having diabetes 
does not relate to a specific physical structure.
The results from the contextual inquiries were docu­
mented in work models to provide a representation 
of the work of the participant. 
2.1.2.2 Frame Creation
The steps from the frame creation method, archaeo­
logy, paradoxes, context, and field, were used as the 
backbone during research and analysis.
The archaeology is in the research describing what 
has currently been done, e.g. current storage soluti­
ons. Living with T1D presents dilemmas which were 
included alongside design paradoxes. Dilemmas 
were distinguished as being the individual’s difficult 
choice between two alternatives contrary to design 
paradoxes which Andreasen et al. (2015) defines as 
“... a conflict between two possible, well-argued, in­
terpretations of a design situation”. Dilemmas and 
paradoxes were both included as they can be framed 
as wicked problems which set the scene of what a 
design solution should address.
A development arena was used as a tool throughout 
the process to illustrate and widen the understanding 
21
==PAGE:22==
of the context for the inner circle of stakeholder and 
the whole field of type 1 diabetes in Denmark. The 
development arena is the space that can contain pro­
cesses of mapping the field, enabling change mana­
gement, and enabling the restructuring of relations 
(Jorgensen, U., & Sorensen, O. H., 1999). The de­
velopment arena frames several complex and hete­
rogeneous processes in technological change.
2.1.3 Affinity Diagram
All statements, representing problematic situations 
and themes, from potential users were written on 
post-its. All of these were organized in an affinity 
diagram (Brassard 1989).
According to Holtzblatt, K. and Beyer, H. (1997), it 
is recommended to include 15-20 people and wri­
te 50-100 post-its per person in a contextual design 
project. All potential users (20 people) were inclu­
ded and approximately 40-80 post-its were written 
per participant. 
Besides showing the scope of the customer problem, 
the affinity diagram defines the key quality requi­
rements in the system. It is built in a hierarchical 
structure to group similar data together and is built 
bottom-up instead of making predefined categories. 
“Starting from such a set of categories reduces buil­
ding an affinity to a sorting task; each note goes in 
its own bucket, and at the end you know no more 
than you did before.” (Holtzblatt, K. and Beyer, 
H.,1997). Some of the statements were highlighted 
throughout the report as problematic situations. 
2.1.4 Need- and Motivation Analysis
Armstrong (1980) states that “all design begins with 
a clearly defined need”. Here design is understood 
as the synthesis process contrary to conceptual de­
sign’s understanding where the steps before ideation 
are also included. Understanding needs is therefore 
essential for developing concepts. Needs were ana­
lyzed based on several need and motivation theories. 
Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs was examined. It ca­
tegorizes how crucial the needs are and proposes 
that the most basic needs have to be met before hig­
her needs (Maslow, A. H., 1943). The hierarchical 
order is contrary to Alderfer’s ERG theory which 
proposes that different individuals potentially prio­
ritize the needs in different orders based on their life 
views (Alderfer, C. P., 1969). It is evaluated whether 
it should be seen as hierarchical or not based on sta­
tements from the interviews.
The Hierarchy of Product Needs (Jordan, P. W., 
2002), which is inspired by Maslow, is used when 
talking about unmet device needs for Functionality, 
Usability, and Pleasure. Thomas’ Shape and tem­
poral aspects of need theory (Thomas, R. J.,1993) 
is used when examining the recurrence of a single 
need. Herzberg’s Two-factor theory is used to ana­
lyze which factors function as motivators for people 
with T1D to work harder on managing their diabetes 
and which hygiene factors there have to be in place 
for them to work on it (Herzberg, F. (2017). 
2.1.5 Idea Loop
Idea elements that occured during the Exploration 
phase were saved in an “idea bank”. By the end of 
the Exploration, a short idea brainstorm based on 
themes and need statements from the affinity dia­
gram was conducted with the purpose of guiding the 
direction of the project. These ideas were documen­
ted in an idea catalogue for later conceptualization. 
Each idea was tagged with its respective theme and 
its category, e.g., marketing, as the idea catalogue 
was also meant as a boundary object for knowledge 
exchange to inspire future projects for life improve­
ment for persons with T1D. A boundary object is an 
object which translates knowledge and phenomena 
between actors (Rhinow, H. et al, 2012).
2.1.6 Design Task- and Goal 
Formulation
A design task formulation was made based on the 
themes and needs, perspectives from the Danish 
Diabetes Association and a reframing of problema­
tic situations into tasks. This led to a system demar­
cation. 
Stakeholders were condensed into personas to give 
a clearer visualization of who the design team was 
designing for. Scenarios based on the problematic 
situations were created to illustrate areas of impro­
vement.
A goal specification was made which includes the 
seven universal virtues (Olesen, J., 1992) and value 
statements based on the Exploration.
22
==PAGE:23==
2.2 Stakeholder Involvement
Stakeholder involvement was done through se­
mi-structured, contextual inquiries with elements of 
a traditional interview. Mostly through video calls 
instead of directly beside the potential user.
2.2.1 User Recruitment Process
Two of the participants were recruited from referen­
ces from friends and two of the participants were 
recruited through the contact person in the Danish 
Diabetes Association, Anne-Marie Karin Wegener. 
The rest were recruited through two Danish Face­
book networks for type 1 Diabetics: Diabetes type 
1 and Diabetes i Danmark (os med type 1). The in­
vitation in Figure 9 was posted to give information 
and attract attention. The invitation consisted of a 
brief description of the project, suggestions for in­
terview format and dates, contact information, lo­
gos of collaborators and pictures of the design team 
to create familiarity. Email and telephone numbers 
were given but it was also encouraged to write in the 
comment field on Facebook. Comments in the com­
ment field creates traffic so Facebook’s algorithm 
considers the post to be of higher importance and 
therefore makes sure that more group members see 
it (Mosseri, A., 2018). 
The participants were encouraged to bring their dia­
betes products so that a contextual inquiry could be 
conducted. 
20 interviews were conducted. All participants are 
type 1 diabetics except one with secondary diabetes 
with characteristics of type 1. It was chosen to focus 
on the diabetics, the potential users of the final so­
lution, since “Users are ‘specialists’ in the product’s 
use” (Andreasen, M et al., 2015). The interviews 
were based on personal experience rather than se­
condary data on how persons with T1D might feel, 
in order to avoid the third-person effect (Münster, 
M., 2017).
Most participants were within the target group, 
18-40-year-olds with T1D, but some participants 
were also older. This was both to understand people 
outside of the target group but also to gain insights 
into the archeology (Dorst, K et al., 2016). 
2.2.2 User Interview Setup
The interviews had a length of 30-60 min and an in­
terview guide was used for all interviews. First, the 
design team presented themselves and the project 
and asked for photo and video consent. The ques­
tions were structured to first ask about the basics of 
how long the participant has had diabetes and what 
insulin treatment and blood glucose monitoring pro­
ducts they use. It then went into more open questi­
ons with specific questions afterwards. The choice 
to start with open questions was taken to avoid pri­
ming. 
Specific questions were added later on as problem 
areas were unveiled during former interviews, e.g., 
“Have you experienced that any of your devices 
broke and why?” Others were removed during the 
process as answers started to become predictable, 
e.g., “How was the process for getting your gluco­
se sensor or insulin pump?” In Figure 10 the main 
categories of questions are listed. The full interview 
guide can be seen in Appendix 3. 
The master/apprentice model (Holtzblatt, K., & 
Beyer, H., 1997) was used so the participants, ma­
sters in their diabetes-related routines, could teach 
the design team how they deal with T1D. The In­
terviewer/interviewee model was avoided by not 
starting out as if it was a questionnaire to be filled 
out. The relation of the design team being the expert 
and the participant being a novice was avoided by 
highlighting that the design team was interested in 
Figure 9: Interview invitation
23
==PAGE:24==
getting insights and is new in the field of diabetes.
The interviews were conducted during a Covid-19 
lockdown where there was an assembly ban on 
gathering more than five people and most people 
were working from home. Only one interview was 
physical and in 18 out of 19 video meetings, the par­
ticipant would be in their home setting during the in­
terview. The participants had their camera turned on 
and were asked to show the diabetes products that 
they use and some also showed the team around in 
their home to see how the products were stored. 
In the physical meeting, the design team met the par­
ticipant directly after she had been at work in Julie’s 
home setting as they were already familiar with each 
other. The participant had brought the products she 
would normally have with her.
These are only some contexts which people with 
T1D must manage their diabetes in. The design team 
learned about other contexts, e.g., travel scenarios 
and pharmacy trips, in a traditional interview form.
2.2.3 Involvement of other Stakeholders
Chief physician of Steno Diabetes Center’s type 1 
clinic, Henrik Ullits Andersen, was also intervie­
wed. For this, a separate interview guide was struc­
tured, focusing on the role of the treatment team and 
the perception of the treatment process shown in 
Figure 11 (full version can be seen in Appendix 4).
A meeting with a panel from the Danish Diabetes 
Association was done as the final step of the Explo­
ration phase. In this meeting, the process and results 
were explained to inspire their work. It also allowed 
the design team to get more input and feedback on: 
•	 Is there anything that can be recognized from 
their insight?
•	 From their point of view, which areas seem most 
relevant to work with?
•	 Are there any areas that they think would make a 
bigger difference than others for the individual?

3 Research in the 
Exploration phase
The research in the Exploration phase consi­
sted of both desktop research and fieldwork. 
First, short research on diabetes was conduc­
ted to establish a basic understanding of T1D. 
The research was then divided into an explora­
tion of technology and exploration of context 
where statements and work models from con­
textual inquiries and interviews were included.
25
==PAGE:26==
3.1 Research on Diabetes
This section provides a brief understanding of type 1 
diabetes (T1D) as well as an overview comparing it 
to other diabetes types. The research is mainly based 
on information obtained from The Danish Diabetes 
Association’s website.
T1D is an autoimmune disease, where the body has 
damaged the beta cells of the pancreas, which pro­
duce the hormone insulin. Thereby the body can no 
longer produce insulin, which is necessary to get glu­
cose from the blood into the cells and create energy 
(Diabetes UK). Without insulin, the blood glucose 
level increase as the glucose stays in the blood. This 
can create an array of complications such as inflic­
ting damage to the heart, eyes, and kidneys.
There is no known reason why people get T1D or 
what triggers the autoimmune condition. It is noti­
ced by quickly developing symptoms and is diag­
nosed by measuring the blood glucose level. If the 
level is above 7.0 mmol/l it is diagnosed as diabetes.
T1D is a severe chronic disease and treatment is star­
ted right away. As it cannot be cured, people with 
T1D will need to get insulin for the rest of their life. 
The insulin-need depends on the blood glucose le­
vel, which should preferably be between 4-7 mmol/l 
before a meal and below 10 mmol/l two hours after.
Regular check-ups are needed. These include me­
asuring the average blood glucose level (HbA1c) 
to determine the needed treatment, checking the 
eyes to prevent damage to the blood vessels, and 
checking the feet. Furthermore, exercise and diet 
can also have an impact on how diabetics feel.
A comparison on all diabetes types can be seen in 
Figure 12 (Worksheet 2). It is seen that secondary 
diabetes (T3D) have many similarities as this type 
can behave as either T1D or type 2 diabetes.
26

3.1.1 Insulin Intake Routines
Basal insulin keeps the blood glucose level consi­
stent during fasting and bolus insulin is taken with a 
meal to keep the blood glucose level consistent af­
ter the meal (Table 1, the Danish Endocrine Society, 
2019). The dos and don’ts when using insulin can be 
seen in Table 2.
3.2 Exploration of Technology
For people with T1D, it is necessary to have techno­
logy for insulin treatment and blood glucose moni­
toring. There are different options (Worksheet 3). 
Types of insulin treatment technology:
•	 Insulin pens (some pre-filled)
•	 Insulin pumps
•	 Insulin inhalators
•	 Insulin jet injectors
Types of blood glucose monitoring technology:
•	 Glucometers with test strips and lancets
•	 Glucose sensors
•	 Built-in sensors (e.g. Apple Watch)
Newer technology on the market includes smart rea­
ders, e.g. MiaoMiao, which are attached to the sen­
sor and can automatically track blood glucose and 
give alarms on the phone. It is not possible to get 
grants for these as the hospital cannot recommend 
them yet. Mobile apps are made for note-taking of 
insulin and glucose data. There are also apps and 
websites for carbohydrate counting. 
Important actors for the development and use of te­
chnology for people with T1D can be seen in Figure 
13 (from Worksheet 1).
All approved medical devices (except apps), must 
be either prescribed by the doctor, if it is categorized 
as a treatment device, or granted by the municipality 
if it is categorized as an assistive device.
3.2.1 Insulin Treatment Technology
To get a basic understanding of the persons with 
T1D’s daily use of insulin treatment technology, the 
activities around the technology were modelled be­
fore the interviews using domain theory with ope­
ration activities, use activities and product life ac­
==PAGE:28==
28
Figure 13: Actor mapping
tivities. This was done for the most commonly used 
insulin treatment devices, insulin pens and insulin 
pumps (Worksheet 5).
The use activities for insulin pens can be seen in Fi­
gure 16. In the case of injection insulin with a pen, 
the user has to be able to see very small drops of 
insulin and bruises are not uncommon.  The use ac­
tivities for insulin pumps can be seen in Figure 17. 
The insulin pump is much more complex to use and 
operate. However, once it is on, it eases the work of 
the diabetic.
3.2.1.2 Findings on Insulin Treatment Techno­
logy from Field Work
The participants either use insulin pens or pumps. 
An issue of insulin pens is the discomfort of needles.
”I was afraid of needles and after 3 years it is 
still uncomfortable to take insulin.” 
- Tue, T1D for 3 years, 35-40 y/o
A way to make sure that the injections run more 
smoothly is to use an injection port but not everyone 
who encounters issues with injections are informed 
about injection ports (Figure 14).
”I don’t have an injection port and actually 
didn’t know that they exist”. 
- Tue, T1D for 3 years, 35-40 y/o
Another issue with pens is that they might break.
“The insulin pens are not very robust. They 
break easily.” 
- Rose, T1D for 2 years, 24 y/o

Figure 17: Activity domain for insulin pumps
There are both positive and negative opinions on 
switching to a pump (Figure 15). Having a pump 
can make insulin estimations more precise and can 
calculate amounts.
”When I had a pen, I took a finger in the wind 
and said: ’It is probably 2 units.” 
- Roland, T1D for 25 years, 41 y/o
Choosing not to have a pump is sometimes conne­
cted to experiences or perceptions of the pump get­
ting loose.
”I talked to the doctor and diabetes nurse and 
got the option to use a pump. But I don’t want 
it because of contact sports and children.” 
- Tue, T1D for 3 years, 35-40 y/o
Having a pump can limit how the user can dress as 
not every clothing item has pockets and a one-piece 
does not allow room for the tube. 
”I want the freedom to choose how to dress 
and not have to wear a two-piece.”
- Hanne Mette, T1D for 34 years, 49 y/o
Therefore Hanne Mette has created solutions that 
allow her to wear a dress (Figure 18). The different 
options for placement of the pump can be seen in 
Figure 19 and are further examined on Worksheet 7.
Another clothing issue when wearing a pump arises 
if the pocket containing it is too loose.
”I just wear my pump when going to the gym 
- though it can fall out of loose pockets when 
using machines”
- Magnus, T1D for less than a year, 17 y/o
Some use alternative placements for the pump, e.g. 
the bra which, however, creates other issues.
”I have my pump in a bra - if the screen lights 
up, people start paying it attention.” 
- Ninna, T1D for 21 years, 34 y/o

Additionally, the pump limits how to position the

3.2.2 Blood Glucose Monitoring
Technology
The activity domain for glucose sensors and glu­
cometer with test strips (Worksheet 4) were model­
led. Using a glucose sensor involves several loose 
parts (Figure 20). On the other hand, the activity of 
using a glucometer can be quite comprehensive and 
the user has to pay attention to blood flow and not 
get the first drop while not getting too much (Figure 
21).
3.2.2.1 Findings on Blood Glucose Monitoring 
Technology from Field Work
Similar to insulin pens, glucometers are not always 
robust enough. The interview participant, Rose, bro­
ke her glucometer as it flew out of her bag when she 
was on the dance floor. In other cases it is damaged 
because it is not waterproof.
”I was at a festival and spilt beer on my glu­
cometer so it broke.” 
- Julie, T1D for 6 years, 23 y/o
Most of the test participants have a sensor, the ma­
jority being FreeStyle Libre 1 (Figure 22, Worksheet 
8). An issue with choosing a sensor is, similarly to 
choosing a pump for insulin treatment, that it might 
not stick well enough. For Tue, contact sport is an 
issue. He has often lost his sensor when playing 
American football. Other times, it is an issue of not 
remembering that you are wearing it which causes 
the user to lose it.

”You have to remember where you have the 
sensor: In the shower, the band-aid gets wet 
and sometimes I push it off with a towel. It 
can also be when I work out while lying on my 
back.”
 - Nicole, T1D for 9 years, 20 y/o
Some sensors can communicate with a pump, howe­
ver, there can be trade-offs with these sensors. Met­
te, who has no issues with her current sensor be­
coming loose, had to use a bandage for a former one 
and it also had to be calibrated many times per day. 
Furthermore, sensors can be painful to wear.
”I have experienced a lot of pain with the sen­
sor - if you put it a little far up in the arm, I can 
feel the rush of a needle. I once had to take it 
out after 3 days because it hurt. Now I always 
take it off if it hurts even though I have to throw 
it out..” 
- Thomas, T3D for 8 years, 28 y/o
Having a sensor makes it easy to check the blood 
glucose and many users say they check their sensor 
like they check the clock. It is especially easy for 
sensors that use NFC (near-field communication).
”It is really smart that I can scan by logging on 
with NFC - I don’t even have to have the app 
open.” 
- Thomas, T3D for 8 years, 28 y/o
However, the sensor is not as precise as the finger 
pricker as it measures in the tissue.

”I don’t really trust the sensor, it often gives

- Elisabeth, T1D for 12 years, 29 y/o

their sensor.

- Roland, T1D for 30 years, 41 y/o

”I got my sensor during pregnancy - I probably

- Elisabeth, T1D for 12 years, 29 y/o
However, it only goes for some. For others, it is im­
portant that people know so they understand the be­
havior of the person with T1D and know how to act 
if the person with T1D needs help.
Shifting to a sensor is not necessarily seen as a shift 
to more modern technology.
”I was given a new glucometer when I got my 
new sensor but it is gigantic - it looks like those 
big grandma phones.” 
- Elisabeth, T1D for 12 years, 29 y/o
3.2.3 Personal Management Technology
Some of the participants used the smart reader, Mi­
aoMiao, which can be attached to the sensor to give 
alarms (Figure 23).
”It helps a lot that my MiaoMiao can give me 
alarms at night. Some diabetics live in constant 
fear of not waking up.”  
- Rose, T1D, 24 y/o
However, the data from MiaoMiao is not transfer­
red to the doctor which is the case with the sensor. 
The sensor therefore still has to be scanned to enable 
communication with the doctor. Thus it is only for 
personal management.
Some of the participants with T1D use mobile apps 
a lot for personal management. 
”I am very dependent on my apps.” 
- Rose, T1D, 24 y/o
Some of the participants find it too time-consuming 
to track their conditions on apps and therefore stop­
ped doing it.
”I used the Freestyle Libre app to note food 
and insulin intake for a short while. I stopped 
as it is too time consuming.”
- Tue, T1D for 3 years, 35-40 y/o
Many of the participants with T1D use The Danish 
Diabetes Association’s app, Diabetes & Kulhy­
drattælling (Danish for ”Diabetes & Carbohydrate 
counting”). It was used by the participants with T1D 
across ages (18-50 years).
The Danish Diabetes Association’s app was used to 
calculate insulin need but it does not anymore since 
it cannot give rightful info for all meals. It is not a 
CE-marked medical device and The Danish Diabe­
tes Association now recommends Hedia App for this 
purpose. One of the participants, Rose, missed the 
function but had not heard of Hedia. 
There are many fitness apps for carbohydrate coun­
ting, e.g. Carbs and Cals, which includes data for 
dishes from many international chain restaurants. 
These were commonly used.
For organising on-the-go, there are already many 
“diabetes bags” on the market like the ones in Figu­
re 24. They are documented as part of the archaeo­
logy for the frame creation on Worksheet 6.
Figure 23: MiaoMiao smart reader for sensors
==PAGE:33==
33
Figure 24: Diabetes bags
3.2.4 Grants
Assistive devices such as glucometers, test strips, 
lancets, and glucose sensors are obtained through 
grants. The availability of, rules for, and process of 
obtaining grants for devices/products depend on the 
specific municipality. Furthermore, factors regar­
ding the individual patient’s disease and treatment 
also influence which grants can be obtained as well 
of the size of these. This can be factors such as type 
of diabetes and type of treatment - insulin, pills, diet.
Therefore, this information must be provided in or­
der to apply for grants.
The specific rules for grants can be very different de­
pending on the municipality - both in terms of how 
much the patient can get covered and in terms of 
what they can choose from. In some municipalities 
the patient can choose between an array of products, 
whereas in others only one type of product is offered 
and the patient must pay the difference themselves 
if they wish to get another type or brand (Danish 
Broadcasting Corporation, 2018). 
3.3 Exploration of Context
In the exploration of context, the design team has 
explored the context of living with type 1 diabetes 
both through preliminary desktop research and 
through the conducted fieldwork.
3.3.1 Preliminary Problematic Situations
Preliminary knowledge on problematic situations 
was found through desktop research by scrolling th­
rough posts and comments on some of the many so­
cial networks where life with diabetes is discussed. 
Extracts regarding issues with diabetes and diabetes 
technology from the Danish Facebook group, “Dia­
betes type 1”, has been used. Furthermore, extra­
ctions were also found from the diabetes podcast, 
“SukkerSygt podcast”, made by two Danish females 
with T1D in their 20’s. 
The preliminary problematic situations found can 
be seen in Table 3.

3.3.2 Cultural Influences on Talking 
about T1D
The context of having diabetes and the use of te­
chnology is closely connected to the culture amongst 
persons with T1D and their surroundings. 
The way of going about T1D is highly individual, 
yet some common discourses were identified and 
illustrated in a cultural model seen in Figure 25 
(Worksheet 9).
Some want to keep their condition private and only 
share it with a few selected people. This influences 
how they interact with friends as keeping it private 
prevents them from certain activities while it also 
influences how well they track their blood glucose 
when in company with others. Furthermore, the need 
to keep T1D private influences the use of techno­
logy, as the private does not want to wear anything 
which may indicate the condition to others.
Some find it important to be very open about the 
disease and even sees the visible technology as a 
valuable conversation starter. The decision of being 
open is also connected to how T1D impacts them, as 
low/high blood glucose can lead to changes of be­
haviour, which they feel a need to explain and warn 
their surroundings about.
The culture of talking about T1D is heavily influen­
ced by the public culture where the media are dri­
vers of misconceptions based on inaccurate commu­
nication, often putting T1D in the same ‘box’ as type 
2 diabetes. This has led to many being comprehensi­
ve about talking about T1D as they are fed up with 
others judging them, playing experts, or implying it 
must be their own fault that they have ‘sugar disea­
se’ - a term condemned by many with T1D.
The opinions and discourses making up the culture 
amongst persons with T1D are seen to sometimes be 
conflicting, creating negative influences. An examp­
le is the positive who treats herself with a new purse 
each year on her diaversary (diagnosis anniversary), 
however, she feels that other diabetics do not like 
that she celebrates her disease.
Figure 25: The culture of talking about diabetes amongst people with T1D

3.3.3 Food Culture
The mindset forming the culture of eating with T1D 
is also diverse amongst the participants which is il­
lustrated in Figure 26 (Worksheet 9). Some eat what 
they want with a strong desire to not feel limited 
by their condition while others are very strict about 
eating as they see it as a central part of managing 
T1D. These very opposite mindsets make the cultu­
re complex for friends to navigate in as they do not 
know whether they can serve everything or whether 
they should show consideration.
The way of tracking carbs also differs. Some never 
weigh their food, some do it at home, and some al­
ways bring a scale. This was seen to often relate to 
how long a person has had T1D as those who have 
been diagnosed recently need to get a basic under­
standing of carbs. However, some, like Roland who 
has had T1D for 25 years, always bring a scale and 
weigh everything during the day.
A lot of mental energy goes into thinking about 
carbs and how to adjust. Some have a need to ac­
count for all food and calculate their carbs at every 
meal. This can be difficult when going out to eat 
where strategies must be found for estimating and 
adjusting.
A common discourse across all participants is that 
carbs should be counted carefully to estimate insu­
lin. This discourse results in a continuous bad con­
science for most as they do not feel they count pre­
cisely enough.
Figure 26: Food culture amongst people with T1D

Since counting carbs was a common subject and 
also done differently, 25-year-old Amalie, who has 
had T1D for 7 years, was which steps she goes th­
rough when ordering food at a restaurant. It was 
done as a design game using Google Slides as a di­
gital post-it board (see Figure 27). Predefined post-
its were made based on collected information and 
empty post-its were available so steps we had not 
considered could be added.
Subsequently there was a field to place steps in chro­
nological order and a field functioning as a trash can 
for those steps that did not apply to her. The out­
come was turned into a sequence model (Figure 28 
and 29, Worksheet 10). 
It may happen in the sequence that the user takes 
insulin at an unrecommended time due to not know­
ing how much the user will eat or if the user is in a 
Figure 27: Sequence design game
rush to eat. 
The design game was done as an alternative to obser­
ving the context, which is suggested in a contextual 
inquiry. This alternative was used, as it is, first of all, 
a possibility during a pandemic lockdown. Further­
more, it is much easier to set up and easier to iden­
tify everything which is calculated in her head etc.

3.3.4 Hospital Contact
The flow between the T1D patient and other relevant 
stakeholders were assessed (Figure 30, Worksheet 
11). Having changing nurses and doctors at each 
consultation steals a lot of patient time. Further­
more, there is not equal access to information and 
knowledge about new technology and research as 
this is found on Facebook instead of through the 
hospital. At the same time, Facebook groups can 
be a problematic as there is a lot of fear connected 
with the other people’s posts about health issues and 
comorbidities.

4 Analysis in the 
Exploration phase
The development arena was illustrated to ana­
lyze the placement of the project and which 
relationships apply in the arena. An affinity dia­
gram was made where the empiri was proces­
sed and problematic situations were formula­
ted. The user statements were interpreted as 
needs in a need- and motivation analysis. 
38
==PAGE:39==
4.1 Development Arena for 
Type 1 Diabetes
The development arena for type 1 diabetes is illu­
strated in Figure 31 (Worksheet 12). It shows the he­
terogeneous societal field where development will 
take place, also referred to as the field in the frame 
creation theory (Dorst, K., 2015) from which sever­
al elements can be juxtaposed. The conflicts illustra­
ted are in most cases related to paradoxes which, 
along with the actors presented, connects to the con­
text of the person with T1D.  Furthermore, the de­
velopment arena relates to Actor-network theory but 
contrary to a socio-technical ensemble, the focus of 
the development arena is on processes of becoming, 
shaping, and structuring rather than only focus on 
the already established (Jørgensen, U. & Sørensen, 
O., 2002). It will therefore be revisited when appro­
aching a final concept.
4.1.1 Relations and Translations in the 
Development Arena
The Danish Diabetes Association is considered an 
actor world with visions centred around persons 
with diabetes. ‘Prevent diabetes and its comorbidi­
ties’, ‘Improve the quality of life for persons with 
diabetes’, and ‘Increase possibilities of treatment 
cures for diabetes’ are the visions of the actor world. 
It borders the actor worlds Danish knowledge cen­
tres, Treatment team, and Social networks for T1D, 
as it is connected to many of the actors present here.
The Danish Diabetes Association is also closely 
connected to the world of Diabetes tracking since 
they have developed an app (Diabetes & Kulhydrat­
tælling). In recent years a translation has taken place 
in the development arena as a network displacement 
took place when the feature of insulin calculation 
was removed from the app, demarcating The Danish 
Diabetes Association from the actor world  Insulin 
treatment. This network displacement was caused 
by EU legislation tightening the rules of CE mar­
king of medical equipment enforced by the Danish 
Medicines Agency. The feature is currently offered 
by the Hedia app, delivered by an independent com­
pany that collaborates with The Danish Diabetes 
Association. 
Figure 31: Development arena for type 1 diabetes
The actor world Diabetes tracking is furthermore 
populated by the non-human actors, Fitness apps 
and Diabetes tracking apps, which are broad pun­
ctualizations, since many similar apps are offered. 
The presence of these may cause another future 
translation as they fulfil the same needs as the Dia­
betes & Kulhydrattælling app, which might be can­
celled and would lead to a demarcation of The Da­
nish Diabetes Association to no longer border the 
world of Diabetes tracking.

==PAGE:40==
Diabetes tracking borders the actor world Insulin 
treatment, as the non-human actors are juxtaposed 
in treatment. The actor world Municipalities borders 
both of these worlds through the power they have of 
grants. The municipalities are in charge of deciding 
what assistive devices the patient can get, causing a 
conflict as it in some municipalities can be difficult 
to get glucose sensors or a sufficient amount of test 
strips for finger pricking.
While the decisions regarding assistive devices are 
placed with the Municipalities, treatment decisions 
are placed with the chief physician, an actor in the 
Treatment team. However, the Treatment team is 
still related to the granting process as medical docu­
mentation must be given. It was found from the in­
terview with the chief physician of the type 1 cli­
nic at Steno Diabetes Center Copenhagen, Henrik 
Ullits Andersen, that caseworkers frequently call to 
ask clarifying questions (Appendix 5).
The actor world Treatment team is central in the 
context of persons with T1D, as it is here the treat­
ment is set and adjusted. However, several conflicts 
occur within this relation. The time available confli­
cts with the quality of consultations and many expe­
rience getting new nurses who do not know their hi­
story. Additionally, treatment options are not always 
transparent or equal. E.g, some are declined when 
asking for a pump at one hospital but are able to get 
it when changing to another hospital. This also con­
flicts with the vision of The Danish Health Autho­
rities: ‘Equal access to treatment’, which is part of 
the National Diabetes Action Plan (Danish Health 
Authority, 2020).

2.	 We have to store a lot of products.
3.	 We depend on technology.
4.	 The technology is too clumsy.
5.	 We experience technical issues with devices.
6.	 The user experience of devices is important but 
not always optimal.
Routines

9.	 Diabetes affects how we exercise.

ledge.

us.

much space.

An example of the section of the affinity diagram 
with the heading 7. Carb counting is a big part of 
our daily life can be seen in Figure 32. The entire 
affinity diagram is documented in Worksheet 13.
In the following sections, insights into problematic 
situations related to the 16 headings will be presen­
ted along with design paradoxes and dilemmas as 
well as need analyzes.
Information about new and relevant technology is 
provided by Danish Knowledge Centers and is to 
be communicated to persons with T1D through the 
treatment team. However, this is often not the case, 
further limiting the access to treatment. Persons with 
T1D instead get their information through the actor 
world Social Networks for T1D, where especially 
Facebook groups have gained importance. This has 
led to translations in the development arena as these 
groups have become an indispensable part of ma­
naging the disease. However, the groups have also 
been seen to provide wrongful information or dan­
gerous advice, making their position problematic.
4.2 Affinity Diagram
The online whiteboard, Miro, was used to keep di­
gital post-its and to make the affinity diagram was 
created to reveal common issues and themes. The 
20 interviews with persons with T1D and secondary 
diabetes (acting as T1D) led to 1043 post-it notes 
(average of 52 per interview). It was built bottom-up 
through inductive reasoning by putting notes to­
gether if they seem to fit with each other in some 
way. Hence not reasoning why they fit together be­
forehand. It led to 16 headings, divided into 4 cate­
gories where the ’we’ and ’our’ refers to the persons 
with T1D:
40
==PAGE:41==
41
Figure 32: Snapshot of the section 7. Carb counting is a big part of our daily life
==PAGE:42==
4.2.1 Products
An array of problematic situations appears in sec­
tion 1. We need to bring products with us, seen in 
Figure 33. Some relate to specific contexts, such as:
•	 Musicals (or other events) where it is not allow­
ed to bring bags but vital products are kept in a 
bag.
•	 Amusement parks where bags with expensive 
and vital products cannot be safely stowed while 
going on a ride.
•	 Going to a friend’s place but forgetting products 
and having to drive back home.
•	 Being in warm weather can weaken insulin but 
it is not known how much.
•	 Travelling demands more planning and a need 
to ensure packing enough diabetes products for 
the trip. Furthermore, uncertainty occurs with 
regards to whether it is packed properly and 
whether the insulin can keep cold. 
Specific products have to be ready on the go in dai­
ly life, which also presents a paradox as what should 
be possible to domesticate as a routine activity can­
not because of the influence of the weather:
I always have to wear the products in the 
same way in order to remember them.
ON THE OTHER HAND
I need to wear my insulin differently when it is 
cold.
Furthermore, to have it widely accessible in daily 
life, articles are stored in several places, e.g. in the 
car, at work, at relatives’ home or in a summerhouse.
Figure 33: Second level of 1. We need to bring pro­
ducts with us 
Figure 34: Second level of 2. We have to store a lot of 
products
Having to store a lot of products (2. We have to store 
a lot of products, Figure 34), presents a problema­
tic situation in users keeping track of their stock. 
Especially running out of sensors can cause issues, 
as they have a long delivery time.
Figure 35: Second level of 3. We depend on techno­
logy
From section 3. We depend on technology (Figure 
35), it is seen that technology is a welcomed me­
ans to avoid problematic situations. E.g., users set 
multiple alarms during the day and night to en­
sure the detection of high or low blood sugar, and 
they invest in extra technological equipment such 
as smartwatches to be able to always keep an eye on 
the numbers.
Figure 36: Second level of 4. The technology is too 
clumsy
Figure 37: Second level of 5. We experience technical 
issues with devices
Figure 38: Second level of 6. The user experience of 
devices is important but not always optimal
For sections 4. The technology is too clumsy (Figure 
36), 5. We experience technical issues with devices 
(Figure 37), and 6. The user experience of devices is 
important but not always optimal (Figure 38), it is 
seen that there are unmet needs in relation to the de­
vices including needs presenting a wicked problem 
that puts the user in a dilemma:
I do not want my sensor to be noticeable. 
BUT 
I want to be aware of not pushing it off.
The Hierarchy of Product Needs (Figure 39) il­
lustrates the analyzed needs from the affinity dia­
gram from the bottom-up: Functionality, Usability 
and Pleasure. Unmet needs are marked with a bold 
font. The pleasure needs for decoration or discretion 
42
==PAGE:43==
Figure 39: Hierarchy of Product Needs. Bold font indicates unmet needs
relates to Opinions and Mindsets. These needs are 
ideo-pleasure needs (Jordan, P. W., 2002) of how 
the devices can affect the person with T1D’s feeling 
of identity.
4.2.2 Routines
There are several problematic situations related to 
the routines presented in section 7. Carb counting is 
a big part of our daily life (Figure 40) and 8. Diabe­
tes controls what and how we eat (Figure 41):
•	 When going to a restaurant, the portion size and 
content of the food is unknown and weighing it 
is not an option. Sometimes nutritional tables are 
available online, however, finding it demands 
extra preparation and conflicts with being impul­
sive. Taking insulin at a restaurant also presents 
problematic situations with regards to discretion 
and timing, as it must not be taken too soon while 
the wait for food might be uncertain.
•	 Holiday celebrations such as Christmas traditio­
nally involve a lot of carbs and alcohol. Thereby 
the insulin need may change with the seasons. 
•	 Alcohol has a large effect on physiology in terms 
of the body’s ability to release glucose, making 
parties with alcohol problematic for persons 
with T1D. Precautions need to be taken in the 
form of eating certain foods, drinking specific 
drinks, and setting alarms during the night.
•	 Persons with T1D have to constantly estimate 
their insulin-need based on the amount and ty­
pes of carbs they are consuming, while also con­
sidering currently active insulin and other physi­
ological factors. I.e., the composition of fat has 
to be taken into account but can be difficult since 
it only builds on experience. It is a routine with 
high frequency and urgency but a relatively low 
duration as described in Table 4.
Figure 40: Second level of 7. Carb counting is a big 
part of our daily life
Figure 41: Second level of 8. Diabetes controls what 
and how we eat

==PAGE:44==
Living a normal life while managing T1D presents a 
paradox around the diet of people with T1D:
People with T1D can eat everything they 
want.
ON THE OTHER HAND
People with T1D get better blood glucose re­
sults and make savings on insulin if they keep 
a routine.
In 9. Diabetes affects how we exercise (Figure 42), 
it is seen how exercise poses challenges for persons 
with T1D and requires extra planning and prepara­
tion, as it greatly impacts the blood glucose level 
- even if they just go for a slow-paced walk. There­
fore, everything around the exercise session should 
be carefully considered, including timing, food, and 
bringing products and sugar.
10. Blood sugar levels influence our lives greatly 
(Figure 43) covers the following problematic situ­
ations:
•	 The hormonal changes during menstruation 
affect the blood glucose level, which is also the 
case in stressed or depressing situations. Additio­
nally, for some, it is difficult to navigate whether 
signals from the body have to do with a harmful 
blood glucose level or the current circumstances.
•	 Warm or cold surroundings also affect the 
blood glucose level, making it difficult to predict 
the insulin need.
•	 The uncertainty of the daily state of the body 
makes some unable to make plans for the week.
•	 When driving it is important to ensure a high 
enough blood glucose level to avoid dangerous 
situations.
•	 Sleep can be interrupted when having to wait to 
fall asleep until the blood glucose is at a certain 
level or when being woken by low blood glucose 
or alarms. Furthermore, fear of low blood glu­
cose during the night can take up a lot of men­
tal energy and prevent some from having a calm 
sleep.

4.2.3 Information and Collaboration
From 11. We have a need to acquire and maintain 
knowledge (Figure 44), a lack of continuous infor­
mation and content in consultations becomes visi­
ble. Assistive devices which might be helpful for the 
individual, e.g., the Hedia app or an injection port 
have in some cases never been mentioned. Often 
information is gained elsewhere, e.g. via Facebook 
groups for people with T1D. When the diagnosis is 
given, a lot of information is given at once, which 
some forget through the years. I.e. the effect of al­
cohol may not be relevant at first and the knowledge 
on this may be forgotten. 
In the life with T1D, the main caregiver is the person 
diagnosed which is emphasized in 12. We need to be 
in charge (Figure 45). Following guidelines can be 
problematic due to individuality as the guidelines 
cannot tell why the person sometimes needs more or 
less insulin. An issue is also when the person with 
T1D is not in charge and forget topics or things to 
bring to a consultation.
Figure 44: Second level of 11. We have a need to 
acquire and maintain knowledge
44
Figure 45: Second level of 12. We need to be in charge
==PAGE:45==
13. We need more support (Figure 46) presents pro­
blematic situations where there is inadequate sup­
port:
•	 Getting fired because of diagnosis e.g. due to 
profession or for being out of the office a day 
because of hospitalization.
•	 Broken equipment on vacations poses extra 
expenses as pharmacy purchases outside Den­
mark cannot be covered by the municipality.
•	 Persons with T1D moving to another munici­
pality must reapply for grants. Furthermore, the 
grants vary greatly between the municipalities.
•	 Time is wasted in consultations when new nur­
ses are frequently introduced and they need to 
spend consultation time on reading the patient 
journal.
4.2.4 Opinions and Mindset
A big part of living with T1D has to do with the men­
tality of the person but also of their surroundings.
14. Communication around diabetes is important to 
us (Figure 2) shows how communication with the 
surroundings is of great value to some while others 
find it both difficult and annoying due to lack of 
knowledge and misconceptions. This may also cau­
se problematic situations in the person’s social rela­
tionships:
Figure 46: Second level of 13. We need more support
•	 Acquaintances avoid inviting the person with 
T1D home as they are afraid to cook for them 
due to misconceptions about what diabetics can 
and cannot eat.
•	 Judgemental statements and meddling results 
in uncomfortable situations as others often have 
prejudiced opinions, e.g., on diet, pregnancy or 
cause of disease.
Furthermore, conflicting opinions among persons 
with T1D can make it difficult for next of kins to 
know how to act:
”Sometimes people have forgotten to think 
about food for me e.g. at confirmations. I can 
find it annoying.” (Roland)
ON THE OTHER HAND
”The canteen lady has written to ask if she 
should buy something special - Thanks but 
NO.” (Camilla)
Communication and openness about diabetes are 
important to many, however, 15. We do not want to 
feel that diabetes takes up too much space (Figure 
48) illustrates how it can also be both transgressi­
ve to share with others and uncomfortable to have 
shown visibly on the body. This presents the follow­
ing level 2 paradox (paradoxes with a wider array of 
discourses) (Hansen, C. T., et al., 2009):
”I like that diabetes becomes visible as it 
sparks a conversation about it and can help in 
case of high/low blood sugar.” (Roland)
 ON THE OTHER HAND
”It can be frowned upon to positively cele­
brate your diaversary (diabetes anniversary, 
edited).” (Amanda)
ON THE OTHER HAND
”I do not want a pump because it makes dia­
betes visible.” (Elisabeth)
Some find themselves in a dilemma when it comes 
to the visibility of the disease:
”It is good that the pump shows that I have a 
disease…
THOUGH
… I sometimes wish people could not see.” 
(Mette)
Living with T1D means always having it in mind 
(16. We have to think more because of diabetes, Fi­
gure 49) and certain problematic situations may im­
pact possibilities of the future:
•	 Moving to another country is difficult and 
expensive because of grants.
•	 Some jobs are not possible when having diabe­
tes, such as being within the military, police, or 
being a bus- or train driver.
•	 Becoming pregnant involves thorough planning 
Figure 47: Second level of 14. Communication around 
diabetes is important to us
45
Figure 48: Second level of 15. We do not want to feel 
that diabetes takes up too much space
==PAGE:46==
and extensive supervision of blood glucose le­
vels, making it burdensome.
Other problematic situations occur on a daily basis 
such as having low blood glucose while also having 
to care for a baby, being in new situations away 
from known surroundings and routines, or being 
questioned in the airport due to the need of bringing 
unusual products.
A wicked problem, is balancing how serious the con­
versations around diabetes should be:
”It is nice to think of it as more than a disea­
se - it shouldn’t all be medicine and disease” 
(Signe)
ON THE OTHER HAND
”It is important to talk about concerns with 
family.” (Signe)
This can make it problematic for some to navigate 
when it is appropriate to bring up serious subjects, 
also in the company of other persons with T1D.
Figure 49: Second level of 16. We have to think more 
because of diabetes
4.2.5 Overall Need Analysis 
The overall needs are summarized in Maslow’s Hie­
rarchy of Needs in Figure 50. According to Maslow, 
a lower need has to be fulfilled before a higher need 
can be fulfilled. However, there are contradictory 
theories such as Alderfer’s ERG theory.
ERG theory stands for: 
•	
Existence (Covers Physiological and Safety ne­
eds)
•	
Relatedness (Covers Love and belonging and 
Esteem needs)
•	
Growth (Covers Self-actualization needs)
Alderfer argues that different individuals potentially 
prioritize the needs in different orders based on their 
life views.
During the interviews, some of the participants also 
expressed that the most difficult is dealing with mis­
understanding even though they have health needs 
that are more basic. One even said that as a child 
she wished she had cancer instead. This was since a 
girl in her class got it and all of her classmates knew 
what it meant as well as how to approach the girl 
and deal with it. 
In Erikson’s stages of psychosocial development, 
the struggle for young adults (adults up to the age 
of 40) is on intimacy versus isolation. Young adults 
still have an eagerness to blend their identities with 
their friends because they want to fit in, and they 
need intimacy in order not to feel isolated (Erikson, 
E. H., 1966). Persons with T1D can also be likely to 
put themselves in unsafe situations due to needs for 
belonging and esteem. Some work against existen­
46
ce-needs by not fixing their blood glucose because 
of the esteem-need of being discreet. Some eat dif­
ferently in relatedness with others, although it will 
impact their blood glucose.
This suggests that it is not necessary to work from 
the bottom up of Maslow’s Hierarchy of Needs, 
when holistically working towards an improved life 
quality for persons with T1D. Solving needs higher 
up in the hierarchy can lead to ensuring fulfilment 
of lower needs.
Additionally, it is identified that the fulfilment of 
existence-needs is greatly influenced by individual 
motivation. Some types of information and colla­
boration encourage the participants to work harder 
(motivators) while hygiene factors which makes 
them unmotivated if they are not available. This is 
summarized in Table 5.
==PAGE:47==
Figure 50: Overall need analysis

and research.

one (or fitting to needs).

amongst hospital personnel.

ne interest and want to learn.
Having responsibility for others.

factors

employees.

4.3 The Three System Levels
In this project, changes will be made to a system 
comprising people, processes, and technology (El­
liott et al., 2007). A concept will be developed to 
improve the current situation. As this concept will 
be part of a larger, complex socio-technical system, 
three system levels should be considered when de­
signing (Maier & Oehmen, 2020).
 
The design team will develop the ‘artefact’ which 
makes up the Artefact Level and as described in Fi­
gure 51, it can take many forms which has not been 
pre-defined. 
The artefact is developed for persons with T1D and 
its functions and behavior must fit into this context 
as well as the context of The Danish Diabetes As­
sociation, who must take part in maintaining the ar­
tefact. Together these make up the Complex System 
Level.
At the Engineering System Level the artefact is seen 
on a large-scale, socio-technical level, which is “a 
class of systems characterized by a high degree of 
technical complexity, social intricacy, and elabora­
te processes, aimed at fulfilling important functions 
in society.” (de Weck et al. 2011). The Engineering 
System Level comprises type 1 diabetes in relation 
to the Danish society as part of the Danish healt­
hcare system including the discourses surrounding 
the disease, which the artefact must either fit into or 
change.
The concept to be developed may emerge from pro­
blems or needs at all three system levels, however, 
Figure 51: The Three System Levels
all levels must be considered to determine which 
intervention (i.e. new design or redesign) is best to 
address the need. 
==PAGE:49==
The design team went through a short idea 
loop as part of the exploration of the feed 
chain Technology and idea elements (Andrea­
sen, M. M., Hansen, C. T., Cash, P., 2015). Ideas 
occurred in a loop during the exploration. A 
structured idea brainstorming activity was con­
ducted to collect the ideas and create even 
more. This activity was used as a way to assess 
the potential of the areas found in the affinity 
diagram and thereby scope down to a specific 
interest area. 
Furthermore, the ideas were collected in an 
idea catalogue which serves two purposes:
1.	 Broad inspiration for the coming concept 
synthesis, including areas that might not 
have been thought of if only brainstorming 
after choosing one area.
2.	 A collection of potential initiatives for The 
Danish Diabetes Association to work on or 
initiate with other projects.

on the 16 sections of the affinity diagram. 15 mi­
nutes were used per section to look through all the 
related post-its in Miro and place ideas on a physical 
post-it wall. The brainstorming session resulted in 
109 ideas across the 16 sections. The ideas created 
for 10. Blood sugar levels influence our lives greatly 
can be seen in Figure 52.

dance with ‘Ease of implementation’ (x-axis) and 
‘Level of innovation/impact’ (y-axis). The ones 
considered as most ‘Easy to implement’ were placed 
furthest out on the x-axis and ones considered as the 
most ‘Innovative’ furthest up on the y-axis - as seen 
in Figure 53 and 54 (Worksheet 15).
