6 Design- and Goal Formulation 
in the Exploration Phase
52
The design team defined the Design task and 
Goal formulation based on insights from the 
Exploration. Scenarios were used to show re­
presentations of the problematic situation 
which the design team seeks to improve and 
personas were used to keep the various user 
needs in mind.
==PAGE:53==
6.1 Design Task Formulation
Design task formulation is an agreement between 
the design team and the client. It states what is to 
be created, under what conditions, to be utilized in 
what way by the user, and the association, and lea­
ding to what satisfaction and economic results (An­
dreasen, M. M., et al., 2015).
6.1.1 Perspectives from the Danish 
Diabetes Association
A panel of stakeholders from the Danish Diabetes 
Association was involved in a meeting, where the 
findings of the research and analysis were presen­
ted and discussed. The panel consisted of employees 
from different parts of the organization:
•	 Counselling & Volunteering: Lene Kølle, a nurse 
in counselling with many years of practical expe­
rience of treating T1D and T2D patients
•	 Political secretariat: Maria Iversen
•	 Communication: Peter Pears and Therese Werner
•	 Research & Analytics: Anne-Marie Wegener
The involved stakeholders were not surprised by the 
many problematic areas found, however, to them 
none of the areas could be said to be more important 
than others as an improvement in any area in their 
opinion would improve the life of persons with T1D.
During the discussions, a recurring subject was how 
much space T1D takes up in daily life with the con­
stant management, routines, and worries. Living 
with T1D is often referred to as always carrying this 
heavy invisible baggage and the Danish Diabetes 
Association had at one point made a campaign whe­
re persons with T1D had to draw a line on their hand 
every time they made a diabetes decision during a 
day. This campaign was also mentioned by one of 
the interviewees, Roland, who had put 35 lines in 
just one day.
It was additionally discussed how many of the found 
problematic areas had to do with the unpredictabi­
lity of the disease and how the appreciation of te­
chnology in many cases has to do with the fact that 
it provides comfort.
From this, an interesting angle of the problematic 
areas is to create concepts that eliminates or decrea­
ses the unpredictability and thereby lightens the bur­
den of the invisible baggage.
6.1.2 Re-framing of Problematic 
Situations into Tasks
Concurrently with preparing and conducting the 
workshop with the Danish Diabetes Association, 
the design team mapped out the found problematic 
situations together with the sections of the affinity 
diagram to then re-frame them into tasks:
Predictability:
•	 Make it easier/quicker to control blood sugar
•	 A better approach for exercising
•	 Create more discreet blood sugar tracking
•	 Better carb tracking
•	 Better management of product stock
•	 Improve timing of insulin dose
Communication with surroundings:
•	 Get more out of the knowledge of others
•	 Better T1D communication in surroundings
•	 Provide quick and easy access to knowledge
•	 Individualize hospital treatment
Product improvements:
•	 Optimize way of bringing products
•	 Provide secure packing of products
Each framing is based on one or more problematic 
situations, which can be seen in Worksheet 14.
6.1.3 Task in Scope for Concept 
Synthesis
The aim of the project is to develop concepts that 
improve the life of type 1 diabetics. It is wanted to 
create a substantial impact in the daily life of per­
sons with T1D, as fieldwork revealed how the con­
dition by its nature requires constant effort and men­
tal energy to manage. 
The task in scope can be formulated as:
The design team will create concepts that im­
prove the life of type 1 diabetics by increasing 
predictability in daily life while taking individu­
ality and a variety of physiological states into 
account. The concepts will aid the person with 
type 1 diabetes in managing their disease and 
function as a boundary object which benefits 
the collaboration between the person and their 
surroundings.
53
==PAGE:54==
6.1.4 Specifying the Target Group
The target group has up until now been specified as 
18-40-year-olds with T1D. As the task at hand has 
been focussed towards increasing predictability, it 
is chosen to specify the target group additionally to 
persons who have T1D and needs help some time 
after getting used to new routines. This choice is ba­
sed on the identified differences between this target 
group and newly diagnosed, for whom the unpredic­
tability lies within the basics of getting used to their 
new situation. Furthermore, the treatment process 
is also changing within the first period, where there 
are more hospital visits and a focus on learning the 
fundamentals.
6.1.5 System Demarcation
A large area has been investigated in the exploration 
phase. While the development arena frames the he­
terogeneous societal field where development will 
take place, system demarcation from the situational 
analysis is  used to describe the current state and the 
system in scope for concept synthesis (Züst & Trox­
ler, 2006). However, the development arena can 
also be argued to be a situational map of the current 
state. The system demarcation seen in Figure 56 is 
therefore used to describe where the formulated task 
will intervene and its area of effect.
The task is formulated with 18-40-year-olds who 
are accustomed to T1D and within the context of 
the Danish Diabetes Association and the Treatment 
team. This has therefore been demarcated as the sy­
stem of intervention. Furthermore, it is identified 
that the interventions made in the system will have a 
larger area of effect:
•	 Friends and family, who must be taken into con­
sideration during development to account for 
discreetness as well as the discourses surroun­
ding T1D.
•	 Persons with T1D outside of the target group, as 
a good solution, needs to have the potential to 
have a broader impact.
•	 Danish knowledge centres as a solution that in­
creases predictability can add value in the form 
of extended knowledge on the so far unpredic­
table aspects of the disease.
The environment is populated by Social networks, 
Danish Medicine Agency, Danish Health Authoriti­
es, Municipalities, and Pharmacies which are seen 
as important actors in the world surrounding the sy­
stem as they influence the system.
6.1.6 Prioritization of Themes
The broad exploration of living with T1D led to the 
16 sections of the affinity diagram revealing com­
mon themes and issues. While these all contain 
important knowledge which is to be applied when 
developing concepts, their importance has been pri­
oritized to highlight the focus.

6.1.6.1 High Priority
We have to think more because of diabetes
Having T1D affects every aspect of daily life. As 
one interview participant stated: ”There was once a 
post on Facebook: ’put a line on your hand every 
time you make a diabetes decision’. I had 35 lines 
after one day.” It is, therefore, of high importance 
not to burden the person with T1D further.
We do not want to feel that diabetes takes up too 
much space
It is important to take into account that people with 
T1D want to feel normalized.
Blood sugar levels influence our lives greatly
There are many unknowns that are not examined. 
Giving the person with T1D better self-awareness 
regarding this can therefore yield high value.
Communication around diabetes is important to us
It was highlighted by a fair amount of the partici­
pants as the largest problem that there is a lot of mi­
sinformation. This is therefore also of high value.
We have a need to acquire and maintain knowled­
ge
Persons with T1D often have the experience that 
they come with more knowledge than they receive 
in consultations. They ask questions about why their 
blood glucose acts as it does, however, the health 
professionals have no basis to know it better than 
the persons with T1D as it depends on many factors 
in the every day life. It is thus of high importance to 
keep the the persons with T1D informed.
Diabetes affects how we exercise
Diabetes highly affects exercise and vice versa but 
there is not much guidance on the subject. It is there­
fore considered of high value to go in this direction.
6.1.6.2 Medium Priority
We need to be in charge and We need more support
Part of what makes solutions for diabetes into a 
wicked problem is that the condition is very indivi­
dual and that help from others is also important. It is 
therefore prioritized lower to focus on one of these.
Diabetes controls what and how we eat and Carb 
counting is a big part of our daily life
There is already a lot of focus on carb counting and 
many solutions. 
“We experience technical issues with devices”, 
“The technology is too clumsy”,  “User experien­
ce of devices is important but not always optimal,” 
and “We depend on technology”
It is limited how much the design team in collabo­
ration with The Danish Diabetes Association can 
impact the devices. Solutions within this space can 
add high value but also has a high risk of being a 
makeshift solution.
6.1.6.3 Low Priority
We have to store a lot of products
It is often prefered to keep products stored in their 
original packaging. It is therefore of low priority.
We need to bring products with us
There are already a lot of bag solutions. It is there­
fore considered a red ocean and would not create 
much value.

6.3 Scenarios
Scenarios were created for the problematic situa­
tions related to unpredictability identified during 
fieldwork. The scenarios represent the user’s per­
spective of current problematic situations. The con­
cept has to improve some of these scenarios. 
6.3.1 Scenario 1: The Stressful Exam Pe­
riod
Sofie uses all of her time these weeks on studying 
for exams (Figure 63). One of the exams makes 
her especially nervous as she feels unprepared for 
it. She has never felt this nervous about an exam 
before. The exam occupies all of her thoughts and 
she does not leave space to take care of her physical 
and mental health. Her blood glucose level increases 
because of the stress and she has a hard time getting 
it down as she has other things on her mind and neg­
lects to count. She sees it keeps increasing and takes 
some insulin but is unsure how much is appropriate. 
Furthermore, she finds it difficult to know how 
much the stress is making it increase or whether it is 
also because of her neglecting to count and her habit 
of stress eating. During the exam period, Sofie eats 
more chocolate than usual. She constantly has a treat 
by the hand and eats fast as she needs the feeling of 
keeping busy yet procrastinating. She immediately 
feels it in her blood glucose level. The fluctuations 
make her feel nauseous and give her a big headache. 
The sickness makes it difficult for her to focus and 
as the exam reading gets less productive, the stress 
levels increase. It is an evil circle created by her dif­
ficulties with dealing with the stress fluctuations.
6.3.2 Scenario 2: Staying in Shape
Rasmus likes to stay in shape and believes it helps 
his diabetes (Figure 64). He plans on going for a 
run tomorrow and has decided to do it in the mor­
ning before work as he feels best in control before 
breakfast and with no active insulin - running with 
active insulin makes him nervous. By afternoon he 
begins reducing his insulin to prepare a higher blood 
glucose level. However, he is unsure of how much 
he should reduce it, as he also wants to add in some 
strength training at the outdoor facilities two thirds 
through the run. He normally goes for a rather high 
blood glucose level when running to avoid getting 
low but on the other hand strength training often re­
quires a much lower level because of the intensity. 
Rasmus likes being in control and finds it frustrating 
to not know how to prepare optimally for this kind 
of workout. He decides to put his insulin to 95% 
instead of 90% which he would otherwise do.
This evening Rasmus is eating at his parent’s pla­
ce but is having rye bread instead of the lasagna 
his mother prepared, as he wants to fuel up with 
long-lasting carbs and only has experience with rye 
bread. In the morning, he reduces his insulin to 80%, 
gets into his running gear, and finds his fanny pack 
for his pump and dextrose. He also finds a couple 
of juices and squeezes them into the side pockets of 
his tights. Even though they are heavy to carry, he 
does not dare to leave without. He checks his blood 
glucose level one last time before taking off, phone 
in hand. 
While running, he scans his sensor every 5 minutes. 
After 5 kilometres, the blood glucose is all the way 
down to 4 and he quickly decides to grab a juice. Af­
ter 6 kilometers he arrives at the training facilities. 
The intense strength training makes his blood glu­
cose increase quickly and as it hits 10, he begins to 
58
Figure 63: ’The stressful exam period’ scenario
==PAGE:59==
worry. He shortens the workout and tries to decrea­
se the blood glucose level by slowly jogging home. 
It helps, but when he gets home he is still unsure 
whether he should set the pump back to 100% and 
eat or whether he should wait a bit, as it can be very 
different how the body reacts after a workout. He 
decides to set the pump back to normal and to check 
the blood glucose often the next couple of hours.

with Friends
Christina is going to a park to enjoy the warm, sun­
ny weather with friends who do not know she has 
diabetes (Figure 65). She prefers to keep it that way

out new things.

place after work and join in for dinner (Figure 67).

6.3.4 Scenario 4: Trying to go to Bed
Louise is tired and wants to go to sleep (Figure 66). 
She checks her blood glucose but it is too low for 
lasting the night. She eats and waits an hour for the 
blood glucose level to rise. As she is tired and just 
wants to sleep, this hour is not productive. She is 
streaming a TV episode to try to kill the waiting 
time and to stay awake. When the hour has gone by, 
she measures again and now her blood glucose is 
too high.

Henrik checks his blood glucose when he gets off 
work and concludes that it is too low and it would 
be risky to drive. He then eats something and waits 
for it to increase. Henrik calls his friend who gets 
irritated as he has rushed home, expecting Henrik 
to be on time and does not understand why Henrik 
cannot just drive. Henrik replies that he cannot plan 
his weeks like others as he cannot know how he will 
feel each day. 
Henrik is annoyed by the lack of understanding. He 
keeps waiting until he is okay to finally drive to his 
friend. They meet each other with a sense of conflict 
since they do not feel that their individual needs are 
met, understood and respected. That was not what 
Henrik had looked forward to. He is frustrated with 
the situation and by the fact that it is common for 
him to not feel understood by others.
6.4 Goal Formulation
The goal is to:
Increase predictability in the daily life of young 
adults who have been diagnosed with T1D and 
are accustomed to the routines of the disease.
Apart from this, several requirements, which a so­
lution must fulfil, as well as criteria, defining ‘the 
good solution’, are specified in a goal specification 
(Tables 6-8). The requirements and criteria are ar­
ticulated for each of the three system levels descri­
bed in Section 4.3 and are categorized according to 
the universal virtues (cost, quality, time, efficiency, 
flexibility, risk, and environmental effects) which, 
as for all design projects, should be considered.
The majority of the articulated requirements and cri­
teria apply to the Complex System Level and relate 
to how the solution must or should improve the con­
text of living with T1D and personal management 
of the disease in regards to both quality, efficiency, 
and risk.
At the Engineering System Level focus is on T1D in 
relation to the Danish society including the health­
care system, why a requirement is that the solution 
must communicate T1D as being its own individu­
al form of diabetes. The criteria at this system-level 
concern the individuality of the disease, information 
correctness, and ease of conversations about T1D.
As there are no defined functions or behaviour 
which must be fulfilled in order to obtain the goal of 
increased predictability at the Artefact Level, no re­
quirements are included at this system level. Criteria 
at the Artefact Level encompass aesthetics, persona­
lization, cost, environmental impact, and ease of use 
and implementation.
Goal specification, Engineering System Level: The Danish Society
Category
Requirement
Criteria
Comment
Quality
Must communicate T1D as 
being its own individual form 
of diabetes.
There is often refered to type 2 diabetes (T2D) as solely ’diabetes’ which causes 
people to mistake information on T2D as applicable for persons with T1D.
Quality
The less likehood of spreading false 
information, the better.
False information found through Facebook and false information regarding diabe­
tes among friends are common themes.
Quality
The more it considers that living 
with T1D is individual, the better.
The struggles are mostly on balancing one’s own blood glucose which is difficult to 
apply general advice as there are so many factors involved.
Quality
The easier it is to talk about T1D, 
the better.
Often people with T1D avoid it as they always have to defend themselves. Further­
more, some are private and do not want people to know that they have diabetes 
while others are frowned upon for celebrating their diaversary.

better.

Quality

optimised rather than adding unnecessary complexity.

Fieldwork was done as a single round with se­
veral interviewees to follow the methodology 
of the affinity diagram. It might have been be­
neficial to divide it into more rounds and also 
scope earlier on and use later fieldwork rounds 
to dive more into the chosen area.
To account for this in the next phase, the de­
sign team will utilize the planned workshop 
and spend some of the time there to dive 
further into the area. It is not seen as a pro­
blem but rather as something which strengt­
hens the workshop, as it is important to ensure 
that the workshop participants understand and 
take ownership of the areas in which they are 
to brainstorm and work on.
7 Reflection on the Exploration
63
Covid-19 made it difficult to perform contextu­
al inquiries. The interviews conducted on video 
calls were more static and traditional. However, 
Covid-19 has made the general society more 
used to video calls. This made it simple for the 
persons with T1D to participate in the project. 
Thus it was easy for the design team to plan 
many interviews.
==PAGE:64==
In this phase, desktop research, fieldwork, and 
analysis was conducted. It was highlighted by 
the interview participants with T1D that there 
are many factors that affect the blood glucose 
level which creates a lot of unpredictability in 
the daily life of a person with T1D. 
It has shown that it is difficult to deal with mis­
understandings. Persons with T1D can also be 
likely to put themselves in unsafe situations 
due to needs for belonging and esteem. Some 
eat differently at social gatherings, although 
it will impact their blood glucose. It has been 
identified that the fulfilment of existence needs 
is greatly influenced by individual motivation. 
If the person with T1D had more predictabi­
lity, they would be less likely to end in dan­
gerous situations. The unpredictability is also 
larger when exercising and in scenarios which 
can cause stress, e.g., an exam period. There 
is also a need for predictability when sleeping 
and when driving as blood glucose-related is­
sues can cause danger in these scenarios.
Technology and idea elements (Figure 68) to 
inspire the following phases were found in an 
8 Summarization of Exploration
idea loop and documented in an idea cata­
logue which also functions as a boundary ob­
ject for knowledge exchange with The Danish 
Diabetes Association.
The design team will create concepts which 
improve the life of type 1 diabetics by in­
creasing predictability in daily life while ta­
king individuality and a variety of physiological 
states into account. The concepts will aid the 
person with T1D in managing their disease and 
function as a boundary object which benefits 
the collaboration between the person and their 
surroundings. The goal is to increase predicta­
bility for people with T1D who are accustomed 
to the routines of the disease.
Problem: Persons with T1D 
have a lot of unpredictability 
in their daily life and have to 
constantly make decisions 
accordingly.
Need: Understanding, 
discretion and stability
Ideal: Stable blood gluco­
se and erasing one of the 
approx 35 diabetes-de­
cision a person with T1D 
takes every day.
Technology and 
ideas: Networks, 
apps, schemes, me­
chanics, sensors, etc.
Task: Increase predic­
tability in the daily life 
of people with T1D.

9 Methodology for the Concept 
Synthesis
66
In the concept synthesis, ideas and concepts 
are created. The distinction between ideas and 
concepts is that the ideas are also be combi­
ned, visualised and completed in order to be­
come concepts (Andreasen et al. 2015). The 
ideas in themselves, therefore, do not have to 
be new and innovative (Figure 69). The con­
cepts were evaluated by the potential users, 
the design team and representatives from The 
Danish Diabetes Association.

9.1 Methods and Theory
A method toolbox of co-creation and conceptualiza­
tion was used in the Concept Synthesis phase.
9.1.1 Co-creation Workshop
As the solution has to reflect the perception of ideals 
for persons with T1D, they were invited to parta­
ke in the ideation. A co-creation workshop was fa­
cilitated using comfort creating activities, elements 
for staging a discussion, and tools for developing. 
The workshop took its starting point in the problem 
space, thereby taking a step back to ensure that the 
participants had a sense of ownership and were wor­
king with areas they could relate to. Different brain­
storming methods and the 5 whys technique (Serrat, 
O., 2017) were used to stage a discussion of pro­
blematic situations and expose the root causes. The 
developed ideas were collected in a idea catalogue.
9.1.2 Systematic Ideation Strategies
The idea catalogues were used to combine ideas 
without a rule set of types and number of ideas that 
the concept should consist of. Development of con­
cepts was based on improving the scenarios in the 
first ideation round, based on context/type of colla­
boration in the second round, and the ideas which 
had not been included in a concept yet were consi­
dered in the third round. There was therefore an in­
terplay between ideation and systematization (Figu­
re 70). Afterwards, it was considered whether some 
could be merged and the concepts were then further 
developed and modified focussing on the require­
ments and whether the concepts improve several 
scenarios or could be modified to do so.
9.1.3 Concept Catalogue
The concepts were collected in a concept catalogue 
which, apart from having a descriptional text and 
concept sketch, presents the activity- and organ do­
main, the idea with and idea in the concept, as well 
as the difference that matters. Whereas the activity 
domain in the exploration ‘spells’ the activities of 
current commonly used technologies, the activity 
domain here spells the activities connected to the 
new technology. The organ domain was included to 
spell what it needs to consist of to be completed. The 
organs are expressed in abstract terms, e.g. ‘commu­
nication organ’ to be in line with a need rather than a 
fixation on a technological means. The ‘idea in’ de­
scribes the proposed technology and the ‘idea with’ 
relates it to its raison d’être (Andreasen et al. 2015).
9.1.4 Evaluation and Choice of Final 
Concept(s)
The evaluation was done systematically using 
Strengths and Weaknesses and the Weighted Objec­
tives Method (Cross, N., 2008). In addition, potenti­
al users were involved in feedback sessions, which 
provided both qualitative feedback as well as data 
on their prioritization of concepts.
Figure 70: Gradual Identification of the Solution Space. Illustration from Andreasen, M. M et al (2015).

9.2 Stakeholder Involvement
The format of the stakeholder involvement in the 
Concept Synthesis phase is explained here. The­
re were two instances of stakeholder involvement: 
Co-creation at the beginning of the phase and user 
feedback sessions at the end of the phase.
9.2.1 Co-creation
A two-hour co-creation workshop was facilitated 
with two young adults with T1D as participants. The 
workshop was planned to apply the five principles: 
Purpose, participants, environment, workshop de­
sign, and monitoring (Valgeirsdóttir, 2020b):
•	 Purpose: To include potential users in the design 
process to gain their perspective on the proble­
matic situations and to have them generate and 
develop ideas on how to improve the situation.
•	 Participants: The participants were persons 
with T1D, which was considered when choo­
sing snacks to ensure offering low glycemic 
snacks while not excluding sugary snacks such as 
chocolate. The participants are young (age range 
18-25) with different backgrounds (a carpenter 
apprentice and a production engineer) but neither 
have experience with design methods. It was the­
refore important not to intimidate the participants 
but rather empower them and make them believe 
their input is valuable.
•	 Environment: The workshop was held in a me­
eting room at DTU. The room was arranged to 
ensure a safe distance between participants and 
decorated with pictures and T1D quotes to en­
hance the comfort and inspiration for the exer­
cises.
•	 Workshop design: The workshop was structu­
red in a planning table that included exercises, 
purpose, facilitation notes, and time manage­
ment. In Figure 71 the agenda of the workshop 
is outlined (the full workshop plan can be seen in 
Appendix 7). The agenda was displayed several 
times during the workshop and was available on 
the table and walls at all times to ensure that the 
participants felt in control of the plan.
•	 Monitoring: Both the facilitator and documen­
ter monitored the workshop closely to keep track 
of time and ensure the output was documented. 
Furthermore, it was the job of the facilitator to 
continuously read the room and consider the 
energy level and emotions of the participants as 
well as the complexity level of the exercises.
Plan for today
•	 Introduction
•	 Warm up exercises 
•	 T1D in daily life (Brainstorming)
•	 A day with T1D (User journey)
•	 Break
•	 5 whys
•	 Idea generation (Brainstorming)
•	 Break
•	 Grouping of ideas
•	 Further development
•	 Wrap-up

9.2.1.1 Enhancing Creativity
The co-creation workshop was structured to enhan­
ce creativity, which is defined by Amabile (1996) 
as: “the production of novel and useful ideas in any 
domain.”. Amabile (1996) presents three compo­
nents of creativity: Expertise, creative thinking. and 
intrinsic task motivation (Figure 72).
The participants, who have T1D, can be considered 
experts within the domain of handling T1D in daily 
life. 
It is the job of the design team as facilitators to crea­
te the right setting and use the right techniques to 
encourage creative thinking by applying techniques 
to ensure new cognitive pathways (Amabile, 1996). 
However, the design team must also consider the 
Figure 72: The three components of creativity by Ama­
bile (1996)
Expertise
Creativity
Skills
Task 
Motivation
Creativity
68
==PAGE:69==
characteristics of the participants and alter the fa­
cilitation to fit these as creative thinking skill also 
depends on personality.
According to Amabile (1996), the two components, 
expertise and creative thinking are important for 
what the participants can do, however, the task mo­
tivation determines what they will actually do. The 
participants have a deep intrinsic motivation to im­
prove their T1D situation. 
To create a space where novel and useful ideas 
could be developed it was important to emphasize 
that all the ideas generated did not have to be con­
sidered useful as over-constraint can limit creativi­
ty. At the same time, constraints were used actively 
throughout the workshop as it can be difficult for 
participants to be creative if the level of constraine­
dness is too low. However, this is an individual con­
cept as recognized by Onarheim & Biskjaer (2015): 
“having a lot of constraints rendered it easier for 
them to be creative, as “less time is wasted on being 
lost”, while others emphasized the importance of 
creative freedom.”
To describe this balancing of constraints to enhan­
ce creativity, Onarheim & Biskjaer (2015) use the 
term sweet spot of creativity (Figure 73) which can 
be seen as the individual’s creative comfort zone 
and which represents ”the level of constrainedness 
at any time of the creative process where the partici­
pants perceive that the intensity of the present con­
strainedness is particularly conducive to creative 
performance” (Onarheim & Biskjaer, 2015).
There are seven categories of types of constraints, 
which were all considered when structuring the 
workshop (Onarheim & Biskjaer 2015):
•	 Individual 
•	 Social
•	 Process
•	 Technical
•	 Source
•	 Domain
•	 Purpose
The workshop was divided into the three stages: 
create comfort, stage discussion, and develop to­
gether. The purpose of ‘create comfort’ was to eli­
minate constraints in the individual and social cate­
gory.
For the individual category, the perceived constra­
ints of the participants for not having any particular 
skills or habits in drawing were tried to be broken 
down through a drawing exercise.
For the social category, the constraint of the partici­
pants not knowing each other was broken down by 
giving them a chance to get to know each other and 
create an informal environment with room for fun 
and laughter.
High
Low
Perceived Potential 
for Creativity
Less
Under-constrained
Over-constrained
The Sweet Spot
Constrainedness
More
Figure 73: Basic Model of Constrainedness and The Sweet Spot of Creativity by Onarheim & Biskjaer (2015).
69
==PAGE:70==
The whole workshop was constrained with regards 
to process. A specific procedure was set for all sta­
ges where specific design tools were used and a time 
was set for each activity. 
For source constraints, the participants had to think 
of their own experiences as a source for problem 
brainstorming and problem-solving in stage discus­
sion and develop together. In the individual brain­
storming sessions, the participants were guided with 
new questions every two minutes, however, these 
were still phrased openly to avoid priming: 
•	 When was the last time you experienced that dea­
ling with type 1 diabetes took up a lot of space in 
your everyday life?
•	 When did you last experience that your body 
reacted in an unexpected way?
•	 When was the last time you had to postpone or 
change your plans due to type 1 diabetes?
No technical constraints were set. For participants 
who wanted to communicate their ideas by building, 
however, there were only certain materials that they 
could use for mock-ups (cardboard, paper, scissors, 
tape, string, pens, elastic bands, and playdough).
There were no requirements as to whether they 
should communicate by writing, drawing, or buil­
ding since the participants are not in domains where 
drawing and building is a common way of commu­
nicating. 
The participants were not constrained with regards 
to the purpose of their output. Their ideas did not 
need to be of a certain quality nor did they need to 
be at a level where they could be validated.
9.2.2 User Feedback Session
To get feedback on the created concepts, individual 
feedback sessions were conducted via online video 
calls with 10 potential users. 
The purpose was to know what they liked or dis­
liked about each concept and how likely they saw 
themselves to use it. In order to get the immediate 
thoughts of the users and make the communicati­
on run smoothly, it was encouraged to share all 
thoughts. 
To evaluate the likelihood of use, a prioritization 
game was done to get quick quantitative data on 
personal preferences.

==PAGE:71==
The ideation was a continuation of the idea 
loop from the Exploration. Ideas were created, 
combined, visualized and completed. The ide­
ation derives from collaboration with persons 
with T1D.

10.1 Results of the 
Co-creation Workshop
The co-creation workshop (Worksheet 17) resulted 
in new perspectives and depth to the problematic si­
tuations experienced in daily life with T1D during 
the Stage discussion exercises and additional ideas 
during Develop together.
10.1.1 Stage discussion exercises
Multiple challenges were found through the brain­
storming- and user journey- exercises and the par­
ticipants dove deeper into some through the 5 whys 
exercise. Even though most challenges had been 
touched upon during the fieldwork in the Explora­
tion phase, the exercises ensured that more depth 
was added. The challenges can be divided into the 
following types:
•	 The unpredictability of how the person with 
T1D is feeling in the morning, which influen­
ces whether it is possible to bike to work or if an 
appointment with a friend needs to be cancelled. 
This can be caused by not taking insulin the pre­
vious evening to account for a workout or from 
not measuring before bedtime due to ‘laziness’ 
or wanting to pretend not to have diabetes for a 
while. 
•	 Unknown activities during the day make it 
difficult to adjust insulin. This could be how a 
workout is structured, what activities are planned 
in a school day, or how busy a work shift might 
be. Also, activities which the person is not used 
to doing pose a challenge e.g. at a ski vacation 
where the insulin needs to be adjusted.
•	 Not informing surroundings creates unpredic­
tability as some activities during the day could 
be better known by informing about T1D or col­
laborating with the coach, teacher, or employer 
about what is going to happen.
•	 Equipment breaking or running out of insu­
lin can often not be foreseen and may cause an 
evening with friends to be shortened, interrupti­
on of sleep, or it can ruin the next day if it is not 
discovered during the night. These incidents are 
sometimes caused by placing an infusin set in­
sertion poorly in relation to the later activities or 
not finding out in advance whether the amount of 
brought insulin is sufficient.
•	 External factors out of the person’s control 
can be time-consuming, such as dealing with 
grants when moving municipality or having to 
attend non-beneficial consultations where the in­
dividual need is not taken into account.
•	 Waiting for blood glucose to increase or de­
crease can make the person late for school or a 
Covid-19 test, as it makes them postpone driving.
The 5 whys exercise (Figure 74) added to the under­
standing of why the challenges occur and thereby 
also suggested what to be aware of to solve the chal­
lenges. It was seen that:
•	
Communication and planning are key factors in 
situations of unknown activities.
•	
There is a need to think ahead and have in mind 
how decisions may influence the next activities.
•	
The treatment process is not aligned with the in­
dividual needs.
10.1.2 Develop together exercises
The ideas created in the co-creation workshop have 
been collected in an idea catalogue. A snippet can 
be seen in Figure 76 and the whole idea catalogue 
can be seen in Appendix 8.
The participants combined and further developed 
the ideas. The final combinations were named as 
follows by the participants:
•	 Handling in the municipalities
•	 Planning in daily life
•	 Hospital check

Handling in the municipalities:
Ideally, grants should be similar for all municipa­
lities and these should work together. As long as it 
is still an issue with grants between municipalities, 
nurses and doctors should be aware of it and be in­
formed on how to help. The Danish Diabetes Asso­
ciation could help the person with T1D by providing 
more accessible information on grants between mu­
nicipalities on their website. Steno Diabetes Center 
(and other hospitals) could help by providing infor­
mation on the subject. They should have a short, ma­
nageable guide explaining everything about grants. 
Furthermore, the hospitals should work together in 
the transition from young to adult. This also relates 
to the options for grants between the age groups (it 
is easier to get grants when you are under 18 years 
old).
Planning in daily life:
For the planning of everyday activities, a wish for 
information exchange between the person with T1D 
and their coach or boss were highlighted. A dialogue 
with the boss and colleagues should be established 
by making it possible to give a pamphlet with in­
formation on symptoms of high and low blood glu­
cose and how long before you are back to normal. 
For workouts, it should be normalized to inform the

Hospital checks:
Fewer hospital checks as they lack content. Either 
remove it completely to allow time for those who 
actually need help or organize it differently. It could 
be by doing it online every second time or agreeing 
with the doctor from time to time when it is best to 
come for a check again. There should be a plan for 
meetings before arriving, e.g. a questionnaire.

The user should have tasks ready that can be done 
with low blood glucose to be effective at work. The 
user should also be encouraged to measure before 
going to bed and to pack extra insulin and needles

10.2 Ideation within the 
Design Team
The design team used the initial idea catalogue and 
the co-creation workshop results to do further ide­
ation on a concept level. The various ideas were 
combined to create concepts using the scenarios as 
a starting point. An example of ideas used for inspi­
ration for the scenario, ’Trying to go to sleep’, can 
be seen in Figure 77 (Worksheet 18). To prevent pri­
ming within the design team this was done individu­
ally, resulting in a total of 17 combinations created 
as early concepts. An early concept for ’Trying to go 
to sleep’ can be seen in Figure 78.
Some combinations were merged and a total of nine 
concepts were further developed and modified. The 
ideation concluded in a concept catalogue.
10.2.1 Concept Catalogue
In the next pages, the nine concepts are presented 
with a short description, concept illustrations, the 
idea with and idea in, the difference that matters, as 
well as organ domain. Use activities are described 
in concept sketches.

Concept 1: ActivePlan
The concept ActivePlan (Figure 79) is based on ide­
as that aid users with T1D in getting ready and pre­
pared for activities. 
Through an app (Figure 80), the user is provided 
with the possibility to plan activities such as exer­
cising, driving, going to bed, as well as actions to 
get a stable blood glucose level, e.g., in a stressed 
situation, based on the factors which influence 
the blood glucose level for a specific activity. The 
user receives reminders of actions to take before a 
planned activity as well as individual recommenda­
tions for how to adjust the blood glucose level to be 
ready for the activity.

organ
Control organ

Table 9: Idea with and Idea in for ’ActivePlan’
To ensure individual recommendations, the concept 
recognizes patterns and provides estimates based 
upon these. Furthermore, tests are available where 
the user can measure how their blood glucose is af­
fected, e.g., by stress (Figure 81).
The difference that matters for the concept 
ActivePlan is that the user gets the ability to 
plan activities and receives reminders of ac­
tions to take to be ready and prepared for a 
specific activity based on individual recom­
mendations.

Concept 2: TrackSnack
The concept TrackSnack (Figure 82) is developed 
with stressful situations in mind where it can be dif­
ficult for the user to have an overview of how many 
snacks have been eaten or the mental resources to 
research the carbs. Therefore, the idea with Track­
Snack is to make it easier to count carbs while de­
manding less thinking.
The concept consists of an app with food-recogni­
tion (Figure 83) and a food library with content info, 
which is connected to a scale that keeps track of

Concept 3: PersonaCare
The concept PersonaCare (Figure 85) is created as 
a means to get more qualified help to deal with diffi­
cult situations in daily life.
PersonaCare is a two-sided platform app (Figure 
86) where the main user, the patient with T1D, can 
create their own “persona” with characteristics such 
as personality traits, motivations, goals, and frustra­
tions to indicate what is important for them and how 
they want to live their life with T1D. The persona 
can be viewed by the treatment team and used as a 
starting point of the consultation along with some 
pre-chosen consultation topics, which the patient 
chooses before each consultation. The patient also 
has the possibility to add notes and reminders to re­

Concept 4: ExerciseExpert
The concept ExerciseExpert (Figure 88) is created 
as a means to acquire and share knowledge on exer­
cising with T1D.
ExerciseExpert is a learning app (Figure 89) that 
also has a platform for knowledge sharing. The 
learning is organized in levels to let the user start 
with learning general advice with only a few fac­
tors in mind, e.g, what they have just eaten. Step 
by step the user progresses and starts to learn their 
individual patterns by gradually taking more factors 
into account, e.g., what they ate the day before. In 
order for the more experienced diabetics to skip the

an ‘ExerciseExpert’ and can share their experiences 
with others in the ‘Sharing’ tab (Figure 90).
The difference that matters for ExerciseExpert 
is that the user has a tool to teach themselves 
and others how to live an active life with T1D

Concept 5: DiabetesConvo
The concept DiabetesConvo (Figure 91) is creat­
ed as a means to both give general information for 
people without diabetes and to share more personal 
thoughts regarding diabetes with close friends and 
family.

about diabetes and providing general knowledge to 
others.

is the ‘Conversation starter cards’ which can initi­
ate the talk. Other ideas to gain empathy and under­
standing with friends and family can also be shared,

Concept 9: SmartDose
The concept SmartDose (Figure 103) is developed 
with a focus on discretion and applies to pen users. 
There are many situations where it may feel uncom­
fortable to pull out an insulin pen in front of oth­
ers or even inappropriate in some settings. The idea 
with the concept is therefore to make it easy to be 
discreet around taking insulin.
The concept consists of a small pod (Figure 104), 
which can be filled using an insulin pen and worn 
discreetly underneath the clothes for those situations 
where it is not preferred to use a pen. The pod is 
controlled via a simple phone app which gives the

11.1 Feedback Session Results
Feedback sessions (Appendix 9) were conducted 
with 10 potential users who fit the target group and 
had previously participated in the project. After 
being presented with the concepts and providing qu­
alitative feedback for each, the potential users were 
asked to prioritize them from ‘Most potential for me’ 
(1st place) to ‘Least potential for me’  (last place).
Table 18 shows an average score of the concepts 
based on the potential users’ prioritization (Works­
heet 20). The concepts that only apply to some of 
the users, e.g., SmartDose which only applies to pen 
users, were left out of this calculation to only inclu­
de the concepts that apply to all users. It shows that 
PersonaCare and TrackSnack were placed high and 
in 1st place multiple times. DiabetesConvo and Ac­
tivePlan scored lowest and were no one’s favourite, 
though, the potential users were not against these. 
Though it was not placed highly in the prioritizati­
on, several users saw a value in elements of the Dia­
betesConvo such as ’Conversation Starter Cards’ 
or an informative folder for employers and relati­
ves. This is something that can be done within the 
competencies of The Danish Diabetes Association 
and is therefore important findings to hand over for 
use in future development within the organization. 
Likewise, the concept TechGrants Overview Provi­
der was well received by the users who have a clear 
need for more and better information regarding te­
chnology and grants, which should also be consi­
dered and evaluated within the organization of The 
Danish Diabetes Association. An example of a user 
prioritization can be seen in Figure 106.

11.1.1 Results for concepts which only 
relates to a part of the participants
SugarSense Earring: 
The sensor earring concept’s scores varied a lot. 
One placed it in the top, others in the middle and 
others in the bottom. The potential users who did 
not give it a top score already considered the new 
sensors to be discreet. Additionally, the users were 
concerned that it might add an extra cost for them 
to get, however, even if it was free they would not 
place it higher in the prioritization. The concept is 
for those who wear earrings, making aesthetics an 
important factor that was difficult to evaluate at this 
stage where the concepts are not detailed. However, 
the size of the ear flip, like in the concept sketch, 
would be considered too large.
SleepSafe: 
This concept, which only applies to those with a 
pump and alarms, did not speak to its narrow target 
group. It scored very low as the concept with the 
least potential in the user’s personal perspective. 
SmartDose: 
This concept applies to pen users and scored rather 
high within this target group. Tue placed it as the 
best of all concepts. Rose also placed it in top - right 
Table 19: Evaluating Requirements
96
after TrackSnack. Thomas and Michael placed it as 
number three. For Michael, discreetness is key and 
he would not use an insulin pump for this reason. 
If the concept can make it unnoticeable, he would 
definitely use it.
11.2 Evaluation in the Design 
Team
All 9 concepts were evaluated within the design 
team in accordance with the requirements, through 
Strengths and weaknesses, and using the Weighted 
Objectives Method.
11.2.1 Evaluation in Accordance with 
the Requirements
It was evaluated whether each concept can fulfil 
the requirements from the goal formulation to say 
whether it is a solution for the design task or not. 
All concepts can fulfill the requirements except the 
website concepts which only has an indirect effect 
on predictability and safety. In Table 19 the evalua­
tion of the concept ExerciseExpert are seen and the 
remaining evaluation tables can be seen in Works­
heet 19.
11.2.2 Strengths and Weaknesses
In Table 20, Strengths and Weaknesses can be seen 
for each concept in relation to current solutions. The 
strengths and weaknesses of four user-favourites are 
elaborated in the following text.

Weaknesses
ActivePlan
•	 Less things to keep track of in head - due to reminders.
•	 Easier to be ready for daily activities.
•	 Tests require fingerpricking or a 15 minute wait for sensor.
•	 Time consuming to plan daily avtivities.
TrackSnack
•	 Saves time for the user (compared to finding content infor­
mation online).
•	 Easy to find correct content information (compared to ask­
ing in Facebook groups).
•	 Keeps track of how much is eaten continuously (contrary to 
weighing a specific amount off or estimating while eating).
•	 Dependent on large and precise library.
•	 User has to carry an additional item (the scale).
PersonaCare
•	 Fast to take notes (compared to notebooks).
•	 Easy for users to remember struggles - nudges to take 
notes and reminds before consultations.
•	 Efficiently used time at consultations - prechosen topics.
•	 Easy to connect with like-minded people - increases likeli­
hood of being taken seriously.
•	 Includes app in consultation - might make the user or 
treatment team look at a screen rather than being present.
•	 Adds additional actions to take before consultations.
ExerciseExpert
•	 Qualified advice from ‘ExerciseExperts’ - due to levels.
•	 Learning path for basic exercise knowledge. 
•	 Gamified self-observation - more likely to notice patterns.
•	 More things to note down.
•	 Learning depends on user’s own observations - does not 
necessarily help them notice patterns.
DiabetesConvo
•	 Conversation starter.
•	 Less effort required from user to provide basic information.
•	 Requires effort from the user to introduce conversation.

the weighted objectives (Worksheet 21). It is obser­
ved that the weighting does not have a big influence 
on the ranking of the concepts, however, it does give 
a more clear ranking with nine distinctive scores as 
opposed to many concepts scoring the same in the 
evaluation without weighted objectives.
The concept ExerciseExpert scored the highest of 
all in the WOM and was assessed to perform Sa­
tisfactory or better on all objectives. It performed 
Excellent on only two objectives, namely the hig­
hest weighted Get blood glucose under control and 
one of the second-highest weighted Easy to gain an 
overview.
PersonaCare came in second and scored only 3 
points lower than number one. PersonaCare per­
formed Weak on two objectives, Easy to maintain 
knowledge as it does not offer any static knowled­
ge and Easy to implement as Danish healthcare is 
a large engineering system that can be difficult to 
change. At the other end of the scale, it performed 
Excellent on four objectives.
98
The concept TrackSnack is seen to have many 
strengths regarding saving time and making it easy 
when counting carbs as well as keeping track for 
the user. However, the dependency on a large and 
precise food library is seen as a considerable weak­
ness, as the concept will not add value for the users 
without, while this is seen as difficult to obtain. 
The strengths of PersonaCare are that it makes it 
fast and easy to take and remember notes for consul­
tations and aiding in using the time at the consulta­
tion efficiently while making it easy to connect with 
like-minded people. It is seen as a weakness that the 
app is included in the consultation as it may take 
focus from being present. Furthermore, it is a we­
akness that it adds additional actions to take before 
consultations.
That SmartDose is worn temporarily is a strength 
because the user does not need to commit to always 
having something attached. Being operated through 
an app is both a strength, as it is a discreet way to 
take insulin, and a weakness, as it is less discreet in 
certain situations.
The gamified learning path of both basic exerci­
se knowledge and personal self-observation are 
strengths of the ExerciseExpert, though these aspe­
cts are also seen as weaknesses in terms of having 
to note down more things and not necessarily being 
able to see the patterns to improve.
11.2.3 Weighted Objectives
The Weighted Objectives Method (WOM) was used 
as a systematic approach to evaluate the 9 concepts 
objectively based on criteria from the Goal Specifi­
cation. 
Two criteria were not included as they would not be 
possible to assess at the current detail level of the 
concepts:
•	 The more appealing it is aesthetically for the end 
user, the better.
•	 The lower environmental impact, the better. 
Furthermore, some criteria were merged in the 
WOM as they represent the same area:
•	 The easier it is to acquire knowledge from the 
medical sector, the better was merged with The 
easier it is to learn from other people with T1D, 
the better.
•	 The easier it is to talk about T1D, the better was 
merged with The more it aids in informing sur­
roundings, the better.
•	 The more precise blood glucose estimates, the 
better, was merged with The easier it is to get 
blood glucose under control, the better.
The included objectives were systematically com­
pared and ranked against each other and based on 
this assigned a relative weighting. The 9 concepts 
were then scored in relation to their performance on 
a 5-point scale from 0 to 4 with the meaning listed 
in Table 21 (Cross, 2008).
The WOM can be seen in Table 22, which includes 
the objectives, their weightings, the performance of 
each concept, and the results both with and without

TechGrants Overview Provider performed well 
on a third place and was assessed to perform Good 
on both Low cost and Easy to implement as The Da­
nish Diabetes Association already has the website 
and competencies. It should therefore be considered 
in The Danish Diabetes Association to develop such 
a concept in any case. However, it is difficult to say 
for certain as it requires a lot of communication with 
various municipalities. It could be scoped down to 
the regional grants and only a brief explanation of 
the procedure for municipal grants.
In fourth place is TrackSnack which performed 
Good or Excellent on the highest weighted objecti­
ves (weight 4-5) except for Options for personaliza­
tion for which it performed Satisfactory as the only 
personalization offered in the concept is the possibi­
lity to log and see personal carb and insulin intake. 
However, it was assessed to perform Inadequate on 
the objectives Remember areas of concern & impro­
vement and Make it easy to talk about T1D as the 
concept does not address this. The concept requires 
a large and precise food library and a built in AI for 
food recognition which is assessed to be both costly 
and difficult to implement thereby it performs Weak 
on those objectives.
ActivePlan in fifth place did not perform Excellent 
on any objectives as the only concept. It was asses­
sed to perform Satisfactory or Good on all objecti­
ves except for Make it easy to talk about T1D for 
which it was Inadequate as it does not address this.
SugarSense Earring and SmartDose came in 
sixth and seventh respectively. Though they both 
performed Excellent on the highest weighted obje­
ctive, Get blood glucose under control, they both 
performed Weak in Low cost and Easy to implement 
as it was assessed that both would be expensive and 
take a long time to implement. Furthermore, they 
performed Inadequate on several objectives.
The ones performing the worst were SleepSafe 
and DiabetesConvo, which both performed Ina­
dequate on four objectives. Most importantly Dia­
betesConvo was assessed as Inadequate in the hig­
hest weighted objective as it does not in any way 
aid in getting the blood glucose under control. Both 
are assessed as Excellent in Easy to implement as it 
would not take much effort for The Danish Diabetes 
Association to offer guidance, pamphlets, and con­
versation starter cards through their website and as 
the elements of SleepSafe relatively easily could be 
made by the users themselves.
11.3 Overall Evaluation
Though the WOM gave a very clear scoring and 
ranking of the concepts, it is important to consider 
this ranking in relation to the other forms of evalu­
ation. The objectives included in the WOM cover a 
broad area and thereby a concept scoring 0 on an ob­
jective is not necessarily a bad solution but perhaps 
a more focussed solution.
Considering the feedback sessions and prioritiza­
tions, strengths and weaknesses, and WOM, the fol­
lowing concepts performed well on an overall basis:
•	
TrackSnack
•	
PersonaCare
•	
ExerciseExpert
•	
TechGrants Overview Provider
•	
SmartDose
The design team made an overall evaluation of these. 
Two stakeholders from The Danish Diabetes Asso­
ciation were also invited to give input in an overall 
evaluation: The diabetes nurse, Lene Kølle, and the 
design team’s contact person, Anne-Marie Wegener.
11.3.1 Overall Evaluation of TrackSnack
Participants stated that they missed functions in The 
Danish Diabetes Association’s app but these are the 
data that the association has given to Hedia app so 
the association avoids dealing with medical regula­
tions. 
Communication between the scale and app is new in 
the TrackSnack concept which potential users saw as 
being smart and as the largest plus - as long as they 
can also choose to use it without the scale in some 
cases. The potential users commented that when 
they are low they snack too much without thinking. 
Some users said that it would be fine if it only had 
data on snacks as this is where they experience prob­
lems, wthere others think the library need to contain 
more than snacks. For Mette, the scale was a minus 
and considered to fit a target group of children. In­
terest in the communication between scale and app 
is essential since it is otherwise a red ocean.
Though TrackSnack was a strong contender as one 
of the favourites in the user feedback session and 
performed well in the WOM, there were many un­
knowns about the concepts which were articulated 
in the user feedback sessions. For instance, for the 
concept to work, it would be needed to take insu­
lin after or while snacking and it is uncertain if this 
could be medically justifiable.  
==PAGE:101==
101
11.3.2 Overall Evaluation of Persona-
Care
PersonaCare, could be very useful for both the pa­
tient and the treatment team as it avoids inefficiently 
spent time. Its challenge lies within implementation 
in the Danish health care system and getting the 
doctors and nurses to actually spend time on reading 
it beforehand. During the feedback sessions, par­
ticipants who work in the industry, e.g. Nicole who 
studies to become a nurse saw this as unrealistic and 
it was for the same reason that the diabetes nurse 
from the Danish Diabetes Association, Lene Kølle, 
placed the concept at the bottom only outperforming 
the all-time loser, SleepSafe Diabetes (Figure 107). 
On the other hand, Covid-19 has made significant 
changes on the engineering system level. There have 
been more remote consultations which some have 
benefited from. Michael is in a ‘flex programme’ 
with phone consultations where he fills out a ques­
tionnaire beforehand. It works well for him and he 
also likes the idea of adding a more personal dimen­
sion as it could improve the conversations. 
11.3.3 Overall Evaluation of Exercise­
Expert
In relation to the goal specification, ExerciseExpert 
is the best performing concept, however, in the user 
evaluation it performed average. Only Roland, who 
cares a lot about exercise and sharing with others, 
saw it as the concept with the most potential, while 
four participants saw it as the one with the sec­
ond most potential for them.  Three users, Thippe, 
Nicole, and Mette, saw it as the concept with the 
least potential for them (if not counting the concepts 
SugarSense and SleepSafe). Mette stated that she 
does not exercise much anymore with a busy life 
as a mom and self-employed but that it could have 
been useful back when she went running a lot.
Thippe thought that the unpredictability entails 
too many factors to be helped by the concept and 
while Nicole said that it would benefit her to see 
patterns she said she was poor at using such types 
of assistive devices. The intrinsic motivation by the 
force of mastery (Pink, D. H., 2011) is therefore not 
there as they do not think it is possible to master. 
However, Thippe has recently asked for advice on 
the subject from a dietitian who helped her on the 
impact of adrenalin (high blood glucose) which has 
the opposite effect of exercise (low blood glucose). 
Therefore it is still believed that if there is a latent 
need.
A consideration speaking in favour of ExerciseEx­
pert is that there are no users who have something 
similar and a simple search on apps do not show any 
competing concepts. 
11.3.4 Overall Evaluation of TechGrants 
Overview Provider
For TechGrants Overview Provider, it is better to 
use the expertise which is already within the or­
ganization of The Danish Diabetes Association. It 
has been agreed with the association that the design 
team delivers the insights collected so far and hands 
all responsibility of further work over to the associ­
ation. 
11.3.5 Overall Evaluation of SmartDose 
SmartDose is seen as a useful and specific concept. 
It has a large innovation degree. The challenge of 
working with the development of insulin treatment 
devices is that it is not within the competencies of 
the design team’s collaborators. 
The strength of working with The Danish Diabetes 
Association is that they have large knowledge on di­
abetes, counselling and self-help. These competen­
cies could be used more in the first four concepts.
The development of SmartDose would run smooth­
er in a collaboration with e.g., Novo Nordisk who 
develop insulin pen solutions and have a large ex­
trinsic motivation to get users to keep using insulin 
pens instead of insulin pumps. On the other hand, 
the SmartDose can be the first step to adjusting to 
the use of an insulin pump which could be a reason 
for the developers to not have a specific economic 
interest in either insulin pens or insulin pumps. 
Figure 107: Diabetes nurse prioritization
==PAGE:102==
102
11.4 Chosen Concept
It was chosen to continue with the concept Exerci­
seExpert as it was well-received by the users though 
not prioritized highly by all. The concept addresses 
an important aspect of treating and dealing with dia­
betes as well as general health, namely exercise. Fur­
thermore, there are not something similar whereas 
multiple food content already exists, which makes it 
more difficult to enter the market with TrackSnack.
ExerciseExpert also performed well in the WOM. 
While this was also the case for PersonaCare, the 
stakeholders with insights into the health sector did 
not see it as realistic for doctors and nurses who 
sometimes do not even have time to read the patient 
name of the hospital record. 
As a tool to master the aspects of exercise, Exercise­
Expert can benefit many users by empowerment and 
thereby nudging them towards a healthy lifestyle. 
SmartDose lies outside of the competencies offered 
in The Danish Diabetes Association while Tech­
Grants Overview Provider is agreed to be imple­
mented by the organisation. It is also unknown for 
the design team, how further conceptualisation tcan 
add even more value than presented in the concept 
catalogue. Whereas for ExerciseExpert, it could be 
combined with elements that got positive feedback. 
E.g., tests in ActivePlan could add more value and 
increase the learning outcome. 
11.4.1 Current Product Idea
The product idea framework, seen in Figure 108, was 
used to unfold the concept in the eight dimensions: 
Business, Strategy, Technology, Product, Task, Goal 
specification, User & customer, and Need (Hansen 
& Andreasen, 2005). At this point in the project, the 
use of the framework served as an overview of what 
had already been considered for the product idea 
while making it visible where clarification was still 
needed. 
As the dimensions described in the bottom half of 
the model had been thoroughly explored in the first 
phase of the project, these were rather well defined, 
while the top half needed more work. 
Both the Business- and Strategy dimension needs to 
be explored further to determine how ExerciseEx­
pert should be placed in the current market and what 
partners would be relevant and needed to enter it. 
The concept was at this stage not very detailed and 
therefore work needed to be done to the Product di­

11.5 Recommendation for 
Other Initiavites within the 
Danish Diabetes Association
Both DiabetesConvo and TechGrants Overview 
Provider were assessed to be relatively easy to im­
plement. The users also expressed interest in using 
such concepts and as the competencies needed to re­
alize them already exist within The Danish Diabetes 
Association they are considered easy wins for the 
organization. It is therefore recommended that The 
Danish Diabetes Association continues with these 
concepts through the development of their website 
with input from the design team. TechGrants Over­
view Provider is considered of the highest priority.
11.5.1 Considerations for TechGrants 
Overview Provider
Representatives of The Danish Diabetes Associ­
ation stated that information on technology and 
grants must be available. The concept can be imple­
mented within the competencies of the organization 
with only a few limitations, e.g. the interactive mu­
nicipality overview. Furthermore, the potential users 
provided the following input:
•	 Signe J: The Danish Diabetes Association 
should 100% just have this. There should be 
some screenshots or the likes to show how to get 
grants.

municipalities would maybe also be more willing 
to not just give the cheapest option. A quiz could 
be used as evidence when applying for a grant.

would be a great help for many.
•	 Signe M: I have always just had the same but 
have not been satisfied. I could have used some 
more information of what technological options 
there were and would use it to see if something 
fitsme better.

guide on what to be aware of when moving from one 
municipality to another, a guide on how to complain 
about assistive devices, and a guide on the rights of 
persons with T1D (Appendix 10).

12 Reflection on the Concept Synthesis
104
The ‘create comfort’ exercises in the co-creati­
on workshop have a childish, playful approach 
that can be difficult to introduce in a formal set­
ting. However, these were well received. Doing 
a workshop with users rather than professionals 
could be a factor which made it easier to elimi­
nate this barrier.
There were no specific organs or functions 
which the concepts have to consist of, as the 
goal of increasing predictability can be obtai­
ned through inherently different means. Al-
though common practises in this phase is to in­
clude a function-means-tree and morphology, 
those were not used at this stage in the project 
due to the broad concept development. Those 
were therefore used in the next phase. 
 
In regards to the concept catalogue, it inclu­
ded many app concepts which could show that 
the design team was primed towards apps. 
Priming should generally be avoided as it pre­
vents the designer from thinking ’out of the 
box’. Nonetheless, the reasoning behind choo­
sing the app format was that persons with T1D 
prefer not to carry extra products and everyone 
already has their phone with them. The phone 
is an even larger necessity for persons with glu­
cose sensors as it keeps their blood glucose 
data. The app format furthermore allows the 
user to use it as a boundary object for informa­
tion sharing. Since there is reasoning behind 
the format, it is not considered to be a design 
fixation which prevents innovation (Jansson, D. 
G., & Smith, S. M., 1991).
==PAGE:105==
13 Summarization of the Concept 
Synthesis
105
In this phase, nine concepts were synthesized 
by combining ideas from the idea catalogue 
and input from the co-creation workshop. They 
were visualised with concept sketches that take 
use activities into account and the concepts 
were ’spelled’ using domain theory and the 
idea in and idea with.
The concepts were then evaluated by the 
potential users, the design team, the design 
team’s contact person and a diabetes nurse. 
Concepts for changes on the The Danish Dia­
betes Association’s website was handed over 
to the association and four concepts was consi­
dered for further work within the design team:
•	 TrackSnack
•	 PersonaCare
•	 ExerciseExpert
•	 SmartDose
The chosen concept was ExerciseExpert which 
is an app concept to help persons with T1D to 
control their blood glucose when exercising. It 
is level-based to meet the user where they are 
and focus on self-observing as blood glucose 
patterns can vary greatly. Advice sharing is en­
abled so the user does not stand alone with 
their struggles and it is filtered to only gain ad­
vice from people with a certain experience.

14 Methodology for Detailing &
Justification
107
The chosen concept, ExerciseExpert, was fur­
ther developed in the Detailing & Justifica­
tion phase. This was done through systematic 
approaches within the design team as well as 
through continuously iterating and testing with 
potential users. 
It was justified how ExerciseExpert can live on in 
the industry by considering three levels of the 
concept: The gold-, silver-, and bronze solu­
tion. The gold solution was justified through 
user validation and the design team’s evalua­
tion on its impact on the engineering system 
and alignment with the task and goal of the 
project.

14.1 Methods and Theory
In the Detailing & Justification phase a method to­
olbox of product design and Lean user experience 
(Lean UX) was used in a continuation of the concept 
synthesis as well as inclusion of design strategy and 
business development to clarify the related product 
idea dimensions.
14.1.1 Continuation of Concept 
Synthesis
As a continuation of the Concept Synthesis, the 
concept was further developed and detailed using a 
systematic exploration of solutions. A function ana­
lysis was conducted with the use of a function-me­
ans tree where the concept is decomposed into the 
essential functions and alternative means for ob­
taining each function are added (Cross, 2008). The 
function-means tree was used as the initial steps to 
creating a morphological chart. A morphology is a 
method for generating alternatives by expanding the 
complete solution space and inducing novel combi­
nations of elements or components (Cross, 2008).
14.1.1.1 Behavioural Design
The app has to fit into current habits and an objective 
of the app is to shape habits. The app therefore, has 
to create a habit loop seen in Figure 109 (Duhigg, 
C., 2014). This is seen as interlinked with gamifica­
tion where nudging and rewards play a large part in 
creating behavioural change.
14.1.1.2 Gamification
Gamification is defined as a “new  technology  that 
incorporates  elements  of  game play in nongame 
situations.” (Prince, J. D., 2013)  Prince argues that 
Figure 109: Habit Loop
it is a technology topic because in most new instan­
ces there is a technology component and it took off 
with mobile technology. However, it is also mentio­
ned that it has been around for marketing purposes 
for instance with frequent flier programs. Game play 
is entertainment elements used to create user moti­
vation and engagement in a non-game context with 
“boring” elements. It is therefore relevant to include 
in activities based on duty such as the duty of mana­
ging your blood glucose as a person with T1D.
14.1.1.3 UX/UI Design Guidelines
The design team followed The Laws of UX (Yablon­
ski, J.) which is a collection of best practices that de­
signers can consider when building user interfaces. 
The practices are based on psychology research and 
are categorized as gestalt, other principles, cogniti­
ve biases, and heuristics. 
14.1.1.4 Lean UX
Further development of the concept was done ite­
ratively applying principles of Lean UX where ra­
pid development is conducted with a Build-Mea­
sure-Learn approach as described in the Lean loop 
in Figure 110 (Klein, 2013).
The Lean loop is initiated by an idea or hypothesis 
about what the user wants. Instead of assuming the 
hopothesis is true and spending a long time on buil­
ding a feature which the user might not want, the 
hypothesis needs to be either validated or invalida­
ted. Here one must ask: What information is needed 
to validate this? and thereby figure out what needs 
to be built and what needs to be measured in order 
to learn. The aim is to “build the smallest possible 
thing you can in order to conclusively validate or 
invalidate a hypothesis”, also known as the Minimal 
Viable Product (MVP), and test it with users to mea­
sure and learn (Klein, 2013).
To learn quicker, the total time through the loop 
should be minimized. Therefore several iterations 
were done along with multiple rounds of (in)valida­
tion with potential users.
Figure 110: The Lean Loop
==PAGE:109==
14.1.2 Testing and Validation
The testing and validation were done with potential 
users applying different testing methods and proto­
types at different points in the development.
14.1.2.1 Prototypes
Prototypes of various levels of fidelity were used to 
test and validate hypotheses and thereby functioned 
as boundary objects between the design team and 
the potential users.
Low fidelity prototypes, such as paper prototypes, 
were used for testing and validating the basic fun­
ctionalities of the concept early on in the detailing. 
The cognitive bias of the Aesthetics-Usability effect 
is part of the decision for this as aesthetics can mask 
usability problems and prevent issues from being 
discovered. Furthermore, as emphasized in con­
textual design (Holtzblatt, K., & Beyer, H., 1997), 
the reason for building a paper prototype is to sup­
port the conversation between user and designer. It 
must therefore be easy to modify the fields to sup­
port the conversation.
The user experience design tool, Adobe XD, was 
used to gradually develop prototypes of high fideli­
ty, where the visual design, content, and interactivi­
ty mimics a finished product. The tool allowed the 
design team to quickly create and iterate the design 
based on user testing. High fidelity prototypes make 
it easier to test specific user interfaces and -intera­
ctions. 
14.1.2.2 Testing Methods
Alternatives were tested in a comparative A/B te­
sting (qualitative) with a clickable prototype. Thin­
king aloud, where the user has to perform a certain 
task while saying aloud what they are thinking and 
doing, was used as a general method for all tests. 
The System Usability Scale (SUS) by John Brooke 
was used for evaluation after the user has used the 
final system proposal. 
There was not a test on the wireframe in itself as the 
focus was on the concept of introducing such an app 
rather on the specific UI and UX design.
14.1.2.3 Realisation Work
An innovation is likely to be adopted when it fol­
lows: Relative advantage, observability, low com­
plexity, trialability, and compatibility (Tidd, J., & 
Bessant, J. R., 2020). The relative advantage of tur­
ning the concept into a business for profit were exa­
mined using a strategy canvas. 
A business model canvas was used to examine who 
and what is needed for the realization of the concept. 
The development arena was examined to evaluate 
how the concept can disrupt its arena. Further work 
is described with the product idea framework.
14.1.2.4 Societal Impact and Evaluation based 
on Goals
The value of the concept was presented in a value 
framework. The goal specification was revisited to 
evaluate the concept on the three system levels and 
possible conceptual vulnerability was discussed.
109
==PAGE:110==
14.2 Stakeholder Involvement
Claus Borup Skovsgaard who is responsible for the 
Danish Diabetes Association’s projects on physi­
cal activity and mental health was involved for his 
expertise. 
3 rounds of usability testing took place with 3-5 
test users in each round. The test participants are 
persons with T1D who have taken part in previous 
stakeholder involvement. According to Nielsen Nor­
man Group, World Leaders in Research-Based User 
Experience, 5 test users is the recommended amount 
to examine usability without wasting resources (Fi­
gure 111). The first round was a paper prototyping 
test and afterwards, it was tested in a prototyping 
software where it looked like an app. 
9 test users were included in a 4th usability test 
since the focus in this test was a final user vali­
dation of the concept. Half of the participants had 
not participated in any of the usability tests before.

from general UX guidelines. The written con­

current guidelines referred to on The Danish 
Diabetes Association’s website. The guidelines 
will be organized based on level (estimated 
from questionnaire) and topic.

15.1 Overview of Functions 
and Choice of Means
The function-means-tree (Figure 112, Worksheet 
22) was used to get an overview of what the app 
should do and contain. It is traditionally used for 
mechanical design. For design of mobile apps there 
are not the same limitations as more data can always 
be added. The design team was therefore aware that 
not all means should be seen as alternatives to each 
other. These were marked in a white box. Some are 
marked with a lighter red as they are alternatives but 
might differ during the journey of the app.

The six alternative paths through the morphology 
provided six variations of the concept Exercise­
Expert, which were sketched out in simple paper 
prototypes (Worksheet 25). An initial assessment of 
the six variations was made by the design team be­
fore choosing three for testing with potential users. 
The following were chosen for testing and are de­
scribed with the main reasons why:
•	 The intuitive to use - to test whether it is actually 
more intuitive for the user to provide the tracking 
info themselves rather than having the app colle­
ct it from elsewhere.
•	 The personalized - to test the learning path struc­
ture against the more locked level structure.
•	 The efficient to use - to test whether it is percei­
ved as efficient to skip the info and go directly to 
the test.
