15.2 Prototype Testing
All iterations of prototype testing which were con­
ducted to reach the final prototype will be described 
here with their objective and results.
15.2.1 First iteration: Paper Prototyping 
Sessions
The first usability testing iteration was based on the 
three chosen variations from the morphology. The 
hypotheses for the test can be seen in Table 24. The 
session is documented on Worksheet 24 and notes 
on main findings are on Appendix 12. The sessions 
were conducted using paper prototyping (Figure 
114) to move the focus away from color choices, 
font size etc. and to keep it on discussing the over­
all structure of the app. The cognitive bias of the 
Aesthetics-Usability effect is part of the decision for 
this, as aesthetics can mask usability problems and 
prevent issues from being discovered.
15.2.1.2 Outcome of Paper Prototyping Sessi­
on
The six hypotheses were either rejected, confirmed, 
or partially confirmed, which can be seen in an over­
view in Table 24 along with the reasoning.
The three hypotheses H1, H3, and H4 were clear­
ly confirmed by all test participants during the tests. 
H5 was rejected as all users prefered the tracking of 
exercise to be as automated as possible. However, 
H6, which has to do with tracking of other influ­
ences, was partially confirmed as three out of five 
participants preferred manual tracking whereas the 
remaining two prefered automatic tracking, e.g., of 
their calendar, but would also like the option to track 
their mood themselves or make notes. H2 was also 
partially confirmed as the majority of users prefered 
a test to be placed at an appropriate level, however, 
one user prefered to start from the level 1 and not 
have neither a test nor a self-evaluation.
15.2.1.3 Other Input for Next Iteration
Besides the outcome described in relation to the hy­
pothesis, the following input was given, which was 
considered for the next iterations: 
114
Figure 114: Part of the sketches used in the Paper Prototyping Session
==PAGE:115==
115
Hypothesis
Evaluation
Reasoning
H1
The user finds it more motivating to learn 
when the knowledge is divided into le­
vels, which needs to be passed to move 
on to the next.
Confirmed
5/5 preferred levels. All participants said that it is a large motivator when they have to earn 
their progress and get recognition for it. Nonetheless, one mentioned that she would like to 
unlock the theory in the next level without the challenge being done in the current level to 
continue learning if she is in a context where it is not possible to be physical active. It was 
also mentioned that the label ‘level’ sounds better.
H2
The user wants to find out whether they 
can skip some levels when they start 
using the app by taking a test.
Partially 
confirmed
3/5 prefers to find out if they can skip a level based on a test. Two participants did not 
want to be tested but rather self-evaluate. One of these did not want to skip levels and 
preferred to start from the beginning. One of the three participants who prefered a test also 
wanted some elements of self-evaluation included. Another stated that it might be good to 
start from level 1 for the sake of gamification.
H3
The user finds it more motivating to learn 
when less text is used and is supported 
by images.
Confirmed
5/5 preferred illustrations to provide information. No participants prefered text as it 
looked “very boring”. One stated that it is important that the illustrations are accurate and 
cannot be misunderstood, as an illustration of running could both be understood as jogging 
and sprinting if not stated explicitly. Two participants also said that it would be good to have 
short text but the option to read more if interested.
H4
The user prefers to have predefined 
answers to choose from when taking a 
theoretical quiz.
Confirmed
5/5 preferred predefined answers in the theoretical quiz. One said that free text seemed 
very intimidating and like an exam. Another user stated that with his knowledge about devel­
opment of apps, he would be doubtful about how it would be able to assess his answer if he 
provided free text, though he did not mind doing it.
H5
The user prefers to provide exercise 
tracking information themselves rather 
than having it tracked automatically.
Rejected
1/5 preferred to provide exercise tracking information themselves. One participant 
found it more fun to type himself though he also found it really smart to track data. Three 
stated that they would prefer if it was automatically tracked, however, it would not be a deal 
breaker if they had to type it. One found it annoying to do manually and would delete the 
app after finishing the levels if he was asked to.
H6
The user finds it more relevant to ma­
nually provide information on other 
influences rather than have it automated 
through data from other apps.
Partially 
confirmed
3/5 preferred to manually provide information on other influences. However, all parti­
cipants wanted the manual option to be available. One participant stated that she wants to 
feel her mind and body herself, though it also could be combined with automatic tracking for 
further analysis which she could add in a note. One user, who preferred automatic tracking, 
would also like the manual option for tracking her mood. Another user, who prefered to have 
it automated, also stated that it would be good to also state one’s mood for the sake of men­
tal health.
Table 24: Hypotheses for Paper Prototyping
==PAGE:116==
•	
Mood or how the user is feeling should be in­
dicated both before and after exercising, as 
the psychological effect of this is important. A 
user can have had a good workout and feel happy 
afterwards even though the blood glucose level 
is not optimal. However, one participant stated 
that mood does not make sense to her and that 
it should be an indication of symptoms instead. 
•	
The blood glucose should be observed for 
some time after exercising in order to learn. 
Notifications could be added 15-30 minutes and 
2-3 hours after.
•	
It would be good with more knowledge about 
specific sports interests or practical recommen­

exercise type when signing up.
•	
Users should get badges to motivate them to 
continue. It could be as a reward for passing 
theory or a challenge. It could also be for doing 
a challenge several times.
•	
It could be relevant to be able to save challenges 
for later if the user is in a context where it is 
not possible to do them. The user should then be 
allowed to unlock the next theory.
•	
It should be possible to get more information 
on a subject if wanted. The theory should be 
kept short and relevant but could have a link to 
click if the user wants more.
•	
The same challenge could be done several ti­
mes so that the user gets better insights into their 
body’s reactions under different circumstances.
15.2.2 Second iteration: A/B Testing 
with High Fidelity Prototypes
The insights from the first round of usability testing 
were implemented in a high fidelity prototype crea­
ted in Adobe XD (Figure 115). While the results of 
the first iteration, such as structuring the learning in 
levels, were directly implemented, other inputs and 
ideas were created in two versions in order to test 
them in an A/B test. The hypotheses for the test can 
be seen in Table 25. The session is documented on 
Worksheet 26 and notes on main findings are on Ap­
pendix 13.
116
Figure 115: Part of second iteration
==PAGE:117==
Hypothesis
Evaluation
Reasoning
H1
The user prefers to have the option to 
skip the introduction quiz and start from 
level 1.
Confirmed
5/5 prefers to have the option to skip introduction quiz. Most participants would choo­
se to start from level 1 to ensure they learn everything. One participant also stated that she 
thought it would be a boring way to start the app. Another participant said she probably 
would not take the introduction quiz the first time using the app, as she would want to see 
what was in it before and then maybe take it later.
H2
The user finds it more efficient to get 
the next question as soon as they have 
answered one, without extra clicks when 
taking the introduction quiz.
Rejected
2/5 finds it more efficient to instantly be directed to the next question. Three partici­
pants stated that they need to have time to think before submitting an answer. Furthermo­
re, two of theses said that sometimes they just press randomly at first and then consider 
the question. One who liked to be instantly directed to the next questions noted that users 
should be informed about this.
H3
The user can more easily understand the 
knowledge given when learning if the 
illustrations are real photos.
Confirmed
4.5/5 prefers real photos. Four participants clearly preferred real photos as these are more 
recognizable and one participant did not have any preferences but noted that it might be 
easier to get a consistent look with animations. It was also stated by some that it is important 
that the photos have the same look to not look like a random Google photo. 
H4
The user finds it more intuitive to take 
the test when there is only one space to 
put answers.
Rejected
2/5 prefers one space for answer. Two participants thought it looked better with one de­
signated space for the answer, whereas the other three thought that two boxes were better 
as it indicated how many right answers there were. However, it was also noted that it could 
depend on the difficulty of the app as it made the quiz easier. Furthermore, two participants 
stated that some may prefer to tap their answer instead of drag it, though it made them 
more active to drag.
H5
The user prefers to have the option to 
postpone a challenge for later and unlock 
the next theory instead.
Confirmed
5/5 prefers to be able to postpone challenge. However, some stated that it should only 
unlock the very next theory and that it is very important that one cannot get to a level where 
they can start sharing without having done their challenges. One said he would probably just 
cheat or close app if the option was not there. 
H6
The user finds it more motivating to re­
gister their mood rather than symptoms 
before and after exercise.
Partially 
confirmed
3/5 finds it more motivating to register mood, as it is easier to think about and one sta­
ted that he thought it was more relevant to his blood glucose. Two participant preferred to 
register symptoms, however, one of these would also like to register mood. The other who 
preferred symptoms stated that there would be a risk of ’over-feeling’ and having both mood 
and symptoms would be overwhelming.
Table 25: Hypotheses for A/B test with high-fidelity prototypes
117
==PAGE:118==
15.2.2.1 Outcome of A/B Test
The hypotheses H1, H3, and H5 were clearly confir­
med. However, the test revealed important insights 
for H1 for which it was found that the majority of 
the participants would want to skip the introducti­
on quiz and start from level 1. Additionally, the in­
troduction quiz was not seen as an optimal way to 
start the app as it made the first impression boring 
and hinders new users from quickly seeing what the 
app is about. Thus an initial hypothesis about the 
need for a function to adapt to new users (see fun­
ction-means tree), which had been taken for gran­
ted, was also rejected. Thereby the introduction quiz 
should be excluded. 
H6 was partially confirmed as three out of five par­
ticipants found it more motivating to register their 
mood rather than symptoms, whereas two preferred 
to register symptoms. However, some participants 
wanted to register both mood and symptoms. There­
fore a combination should be made, though it should 
be considered that the user does not necessarily need 
to register symptoms if their mood does not indicate 
it.
Both H2 and H4 were rejected.
15.2.2.2 Other Input for Next Iteration
Additional input which were considered for the next 
iteration were:
•	
Some want to be informed on why they fill out 
their preferred sports types.
•	
Important to find similar photos so that it does 
not seem like different exercise types have diffe­
rent fitness levels.
•	
Give a reminder to do the challenge if it has 
been postponed.
•	
Summary after challenge displaying the dif­
ference in blood glucose and mood before and 
after.
•	
For some, the calendar does not make much sen­
se after a specific challenge as it does not neces­
sarily influence that challenge. Furthermore, the 
calendar can be difficult to use as the appoint­
ments can be varying with regards to how phy­
sically demanding they are. One user also stated 
that it would be nice with a function to clean up 
the data as she does not always have her calen­
dar under control.
•	
The app should provide some kind of weekly 
report.
•	
Use more exciting badges which represent the 
challenge just done.
118
15.2.3 Third iteration: Think-Aloud Test 
with High Fidelity Prototype
The third iteration was a a think-aloud test of a sing­
le prototype. The remaining features were added 
and the prototype was refined based on the previous 
A/B-test. The session is documented on Worksheet 
27 and notes on main findings are on Appendix 14.
The participants were asked to use the prototype 
while thinking aloud and were moderated by ques­
tions from the design team. Small tasks were given 
to ensure that they came across screens which would 
not be part of a normal flow:
•	
How would you read about the experiences of 
others? (Sharing screen)
•	
How would you get an overview of your data? 
(My Profile screen)
•	
How would you view results or repeat a previ­
ous challenge? (My Challenges screen)
•	
How would you add a new preferred sport? (Set­
tings screen)
One of the criteria on the artefact level was: “The 
more appealing it is aesthetically for the end user, 
the better”. The users were therefore asked to rate 
the aesthetics on a Likert scale (Jamieson, S., 2017). 
The users were also asked to rate their general expe­
rience- and the tone of voice of the app.
15.2.3.1 Changes and New Elements
In this iteration the following screens were added to 
include the remaining features:
•	
Sharing, where the user can filter what to read, 
like and comment as well as share their own 
experiences once they have obtained the Exerci­
seExpert certification.
==PAGE:119==
•	
My Profile, including an overview of the past 
week’s app activities and an overview of badges 
(Figure 116).
•	
Settings, for changing connection- and sport 
preferences. 
•	
My challenges, where the user can view and re­
peat previous challenges.
Apart from the evaluated hypotheses from the A/B-
test, changes included the use of a smaller range of 
mood emojis and that symptoms appear if choosing 
the ‘sick’ mood. It was made more explicit to see 
when the challenge begins and a screen was added 
to warn persons who start a challenge despite symp­
toms or a blood glucose level out of advised range.
Furthermore, gamification elements were improved 
including more specific badges and festive celebra­
tion elements for new achievements.
15.2.3.2 Outcome of Think-Aloud Test
Likert scale averages (1-5) for the tested prototype 
were:
•	 General experience of the app: 4 
•	 General aesthetics of the app: 3.75
•	 The tone of voice in the app: 5
Apart from minor changes, e.g., regarding the text 
and color of a button, the participants had the fol­
lowing input during the think-aloud tests (Appendix 
14):
•	 A home screen should be added to welcome the 
user when opening the app and give an overview 
of data, e.g.some of the elements of the ‘My Pro­
file’ screen.
•	 Option to always add symptoms even though 
119
Figure 116: Part of third iteration
the user does not press sick, as diabetes symp­
toms are not always seen as being sick.
•	 Possibility to make a note each time a blood 
glucose level is added.
•	 Possibility to add blood glucose level after a 
longer time period, e.g., 12 hours.
•	 Short introduction or pop-up for the sharing fun­
ction stating what is appropriate to share (no­
thing about treatment) and that the user should be 
aware that what works for others might not work 
for them.
•	 Everyone should be able to share, as a user may 
want to ask questions. 
•	 Another graphical representation is needed for 
the overview of data in ‘My Profile’. This could 
be a graph comparing activity level and blood 
glucose for the past week.
•	 Possibility to adjust statistics (My Profile) to 
what the user prefers to see.
•	 It is uncertain how the calendar can help the 
user understand their blood glucose better.
Furthermore, some suggestions were made which 
could be considered for possible future additions 
once the core of the app has been implemented:
•	
Have health professionals answer questions in 
the sharing function. 
•	
Include competitions between users which the 
users can sign up for such as “Who can walk the 
most steps the next month” or “Complete a half 
marathon in 2021”.
==PAGE:120==
15.3 Content Elements
The design team gathered information on guide­
lines for exercising with T1D in order to assess the 
amount of content the app would have and organize 
it to fit the level structure.
15.3.1 Gathering of Content Data
The content was gathered through desktop research. 
To ensure correct information, the content was 
mainly found on the Danish Diabetes Association’s 
website as well as the consensus statement ’Exercise 
management in type 1 diabetes: a consensus state­
ment’ which was developed by 21 diabetes resear­
chers from around the World (Riddell, M. C. et al.). 
This concluded in a list of guidelines (Appendix 11).
15.3.2 Categorization
A questionnaire was shared in a Facebook group for 
persons with T1D and filled out by 25 persons with 
T1D. In the questionaire, each guideline was stated 
followed by the options:
•	 I know it by heart
•	 I have heard about it
•	 I might have heard about it
•	 I have never heard about it
Based on this information, the guidelines were sor­
ted from basic to advanced knowledge (Appendix 
11). The order was slightly adapted to group the 
guidelines into categories:
1.	 Basics on blood glucose
2.	 Basics on intensity
3.	 Other causes of blood glucose changes
4.	 Aerobic and anaerobic exercise
5.	 Carbs during exercise.
All guidelines are collected in Figure 117. The 
guidelines which were moved up or down to fit the 
categories are marked with red in the figure.
The ’other causes of blood glucose’ were made into 
their own level later in the process so the user can 
focus on one factor at a time. One of them is stress. 
It might be well known that stress hormones have an 
effect but it is a highly difficult factor to track in pra­
ctice. This supports that learnings regarding stress 
and hormonal changes should be on a higher level.
15.3.3 Feedback on Content Elements
The design team invited two persons with T1D, 
who had previously participated in the user involve­
ment and have a large interest in exercise, to see and 
discuss the guidelines and the proposed categories. 
It was done in two separate sessions.
The participants thought that all were good guide­
lines to include and the categorization and levels 
made sense. However, some guidelines differed 
from what they would personally do. E.g., this was 
the case for the guideline:
If you are physically active for more than 30 mi­
nutes, you may need to eat some fast carbs every 30-
45 minutes during and immediately after exercise.
One participant would eat fast carbs every 15-30 
minutes and not eat after. Here it should therefore 
be emphasized that it varies and the practical chal­
lenge should teach the user to find their own rule 
of thumb based on this information. This emphasis 
should apply throughout the app, as all of the guide­
lines are general and not specific rules which apply 
to everyone. 
Another input was to have more information and 
distinction between types of exercise as different 
guidelines can apply. E.g, swimming requires pump 
users to take the pump off and thereby requires dif­
ferent preparation than sports such as running and 
biking. In continuation of this, it was also sugge­
sted to differentiate between pen and pump users as 
some guidelines will be different depending on the 
type of treatment.
15.3.4 Emphasizing Benefits of Regular 
Exercise in Each Level
Benefits of regular exercise should be part of all le­
vels rather than stated at once to make the user stay 
motivated. These are guidelines such as: 
•	 Regular exercise increases the chance of achie­
ving your long-term blood glucose goal.
•	 Regular exercise reduces the daily total insu­
lin-need
•	 Regular exercise increases the chance of achie­
ving your goal for blood pressure.
15.3.5 Meeting with Expert in Exercise 
in relation to Diabetes
A meeting was held with Claus Borup Skovsgaard, 
an expert in exercise in relation to diabetes from the 
Danish Diabetes Association. The importance of 
linking theory and practice from the beginning was 
emphasized. He mentioned that e.g. a question on 
aerobic versus anaerobic training should be follow­
ed by doing either aerobic or anaerobic exercise to 
see if practice fits theory.
120
==PAGE:121==
Figure 117: Examples of guidelines to cover ordered by categorization. 
121
Red: Moved up or down to fit into a category
Level 1: Basics on blood glucose
•	
Blood glucose during exercise depends on 
how much insulin you have in your body.
•	
Blood glucose during exercise depends on 
when and what you have eathen and drunk.
•	
Blood glucose during exercise depends on 
the duration of the exercise.
•	
Blood glucose druing exercise depends on 
the blood glucose level before starting exer­
cise.
•	
Blood glucose can drop for up to 24 hours af­
ter finishing the exercise.
•	
You usually need less insulin after exercise.
•	
Exercise increases muscle sensitivity to insulin.
Level 2: Basics on blood glucose
•	
Blood glucose during exercise depends on 
the intensity of the exercise (low, moderate or 
high heart rate).
•	
Low intensity is all movement that feels light 
and that does not make you out of breath.
•	
Moderate intensity increases your heart rate, 
but you can still have a conversation.
Level 3: Other causes of blood glucose chan­
ges
•	
Stress hormones such as adrenaline and cort­
isol can cause your blood glucose to increase.
•	
The increased sensitivity of the muscles to in­
sulin can last 1-2 days after exercising.
•	
Blood glucose during exercise depends on 
how often you are active.
Level 4: Aerobic and anaerobic exercise
•	
Aerobic exercise is repetetive and continous 
movements for a long duration of time, typi­
cally of low to moderate intensity.
•	
Aerobic exercise can be, for example, wal­
king, cycling, jogging, and swimming.
•	
Aerobic exercise (of low to moderate intensi­
ty) causes the blood glucose to decrease.
•	
Anaerobic exercise is explosive movements 
and high intensity in a short time frame.
•	
Anaerobic exercise can be, for example, 
weightlifting, sprinting, short distance swim­
ming and HIIT training.
•	
Anaerobic exercise causes blood glucose to 
increase.
•	
Anaerobic exercise or high-intensity exercise 
can cause your blood glucose to increase for 
up to several hours after the end of the exer­
cise.
•	
A combination of aerobic and anaerobic exer­
cise can be handball and football, for examp­
le.
Level 5: Carbs during exercise
•	
If you are physically active for more than 30 
minutes, you may need to eat some fast carbs 
every 30-45 minutes during and immediately 
after exercise.

15.4 Inspiration from Other 
Apps
The design team searched the market for apps 
within the same area and documented it in Works­
heet 28 (Figure 118). There are diabetes fitness apps 
but these are platforms for work out tutorial vide­
os. Existing e-learning apps and the diabetes app to 
keep track on carb intake, mySugr, also uses gamifi­
cation elements. 
Inspiration from popular apps which use gamifica­
tion were documented in Worksheet 29. A snippet 
can be seen in Figure 119. Commonly used elements 
for gamification are: Badges, levels, performance 
charts, points, scoreboards and in-game currency. 
These will be considered as means to give feedback 
and to give rewards which is an important element 
in the habit loop.

telerehabilitation research project for heart patients. 
It was tested on patients between the age of 48–89 
years. The result was that: “The heart patients re­
ported the application to be a useful tool as a part

In this chapter, the concept ExerciseExpert is 
presented. The concept presentation is sup­
ported by a walk-through of the prototype 
screens including the Idea with and Idea in. Af­
terwards personas and scenarios are revisited 
to emphasize the concept’s raison d’etre. The­
re is an ongoing accommodation of the value 
statements through the presentation.
16 Presentation of the Final Concept
124
==PAGE:125==
16.1 Introduction to Exercise­
Expert 
ExerciseExpert is developed to aid persons with 
T1D in balancing exercise and blood glucose and 
thereby increase the predictability of the disease in 
daily life. 
The difference that matters for the concept, 
is that he user gets a motivating tool that aids 
them in living an active life with T1D by en­
suring that they have the relevant knowledge, 
inspiration, and a practical approach for obtai­
ning an overview and individual insights to how 
their body and blood glucose react to exercise 
considering various influences.
The solution accommodates Henrik’s value state­
ment ”Having an active lifestyle has really chan­
ged my life quality with T1D. I want everyone with 
T1D to experience this and I would love to help them 
like someone helped me.” Furthermore, it also gives 
himself a better overview as seen in Figure 121. The 
final concept connects the dots between the various 
factors influencing the blood glucose (Figure 122).
125

“ExerciseExpert is a good tool for getting started 
with an active lifestyle! And I’m surprised by how 
much the structure of theory and challenges 
benefits an experienced guy like me.”

Figure 123: Activity Domain for ExerciseExpert
Motivation is created by the habit loop shown in 
the Activity Domain model in Figure 123 (Workshe­
et 30). The app gives notifications as a cue and the 
user then enters the routine of checking statistics, 
take quiz and doing a challenge. Afterwards, the 
user gets the reward of the visibility of the quiz and 
challenge being done and in highlighted instances, 
the reward of receiving badges or completing levels.

(Figure 124). Afterwards, the user chooses their pre­
ference for how to measure blood glucose and how 
to track exercise. It can either be done by connecting 
to other apps installed on the phone or simply typing 
it manually. The user is asked what they use for in­
sulin treatment since strategies varies depending on 
using a pen or a pump. It is also asked how physi­
cally demanding the user’s occupation is since this 
may influence their exercise struggles.
Furthermore, the user is asked to choose the 1-3 
exercise forms that are most relevant for the user 
in order to personalize the app. The exercise forms 
can then be specified, e.g., biking should not be seen 
as a homogenous exercise form since it can be both 
low-, moderate- and high intensity and both anaero­
bic and aerobic. It is important to prompt the user to 
not see it as just ‘biking’ as these distinctions can de­
termine whether the blood glucose increases or de­
creases and to how large a degree. Finally, the user 
has the option to see a video on how to use the app in 
order to make onboarding easier for the user.

16.2.2 Theory and Quizzez

of using running disciplines to describe the theory.

answers in an interactive manner. The user recieves

A challenge can be postponed in case the user is in a

16.2.6 The Homepage
The user is met by the Home screen when opening 
the app. In Home, the user can gain an overview on 
how well they are doing: Both with regards to the 
physiological activites and their discipline of using 
the app (Figure 130). It includes a display of blood 
glucose levels of the last challenge up to 24 hours. 
The immediate effect after exercise can be negative 
as it makes the blood glucose fluctuate a lot, whe­
reas the 24 hour effect shows the positive effect of 
exercising. The homepage shows the last challenge 
but has a ’show more’-button to allow the user to 
look back at previous challenges.
The user is presented with a graph for the week 
which is based on data from a continuous blood 
glucose monitoring app, e.g., Dexcom G6, and data 
from an exercise tracking app, e.g. Google Fit. In 
case the user chose manual data instead, these will 
be illustrated instead. This provides the user with a 
clearer overview of the connection between exercise 
and blood glucose level. 
More statistics are included to:
•	
Provide motivation to continue exercising, 
doing quizzes, and using the Sharing-functiona­
lity.
•	
Provide a better understanding of the blood 
glucose and exercise patterns by providing an 
average on the blood glucose level and specify­
ing the intensity of the exercise.
Finally, the user can see all obtained and unobtained 
badges.

16.3 Detailed Design Choices
Apart from user feedback and usability testing, the 
design team detailed the design based on general UX 
design laws (Yablonski, J.). The idea with and idea 
in is seen in Table 30.
16.3.1 Detailed Design Choices for 
Optimised Learning
The average person can only keep 7 (plus/minus 2) 
items in their working memory (Miller’s Law). The 
number of facts that are not necessarily thorough­
ly practised are therefore limited before the physi­
cal challenge. This heuristic also resonates with the 
overall concept of organizing content in smaller 
chunks.
People remember uncompleted or interrupted tasks 
better than completed tasks (the Zeigarnik effect). 
==PAGE:133==
The overview of what is uncompleted is emphasized 
in the overall concept. In the detailing, it is made 
visible which are uncompleted using icons (Figure 
131).
16.3.2 Detailed Design Choices for 
Content Option 
The time it takes to make a decision increases with 
the number and complexity of choices (Hick’s Law). 
The app, therefore, takes the user by the hand and 
structures the user actions (learning and challenges) 
for the user.
Idea with
Idea in

human cognitivity

small bits
Make users remember to 
do the tasks
Emphasize uncom­
pleted and interrup­
ted tasks

decide to actively impro­
ve on managing blood 
glucose and exercise

good.

ger to complete a task.

progress in order to 
motivate users to 
complete tasks.

touch targets.

and short distance to 
other buttons.
Table 30: Idea in and idea with the UX details
133
16.3.3 Detailed Design Choices for 
Feedback
People focus on the peak and end rather than the 
sum or average of the experience (the peak-end 
rule). Therefore rewards are recieved in the end 
(apart from those being a crucial part of the habit 
loop).
Users also work more to complete a task, the closer 
they are to reaching it (the goal-gradient effect). The 
conceptual framework of levels provides a clear in­
dication of progress which motivates users to com­
plete tasks.
Figure 131: Use of icons to show what is uncompleted
16.3.4 Detailed Design Choices for 
Touch Targets
Buttons are made large for the users to accurately 
touch them and placed where they are easily acqui­
red (Fitts’ Law). Often placed in the outtom corner 
so it is close to the thumb. 
Focal points are an applied gestalt principle. The 
attention is drawn towards contrast or what is unli­
ke the others. This is used in level structure, where 
the next level to complete is highlighted, making 
the users click there. Common regions are also used 
where the connection between elements is shown by 
enclosing them in the same region. It is used for in 
My Challenges where the information on whether it 
is completed and number of repetitions are connec­
ted to the specific challenge as they are enclosed in 
a box.
==PAGE:134==
134
Figure 132: Improved scenario for combined cardio and strength training (’Staying in Shape’)
16.4 Accomodating Scenarios 
and Personas
The proposed concept is developed with a focus on 
how the five scenarios could be improved directly 
or indirectly. The problematic situation described 
in the second scenario ‘Staying in shape’ is directly 
improved by the concept as described in the text be­
low. It is also depicted how the concept improves the 
fourth scenario ‘Trying to go to bed’, which show­
ed how the persona Louise struggles with obtaining 
a stable blood glucose level before bedtime which 
makes her insecure about changing her routines.
Staying in shape
Rasmus plans to go for a run tomorrow and wants to 
add some strength training at the outdoor facilities 
on the route. From his learning in ExerciseExpert, 
he knows that this workout will be a combination 
of aerobic and anaerobic exercise. He opens the 
app and looks at his past activities to be reminded 
of what he can expect. He notices that the last few 
times he did this workout his blood glucose decrea­
sed a bit but then increased quickly once he started 
the strength training. He also looks at his food notes 
and is reassured that he will be fine if eating regular 
dinner. Rasmus decides to not reduce his insulin in 
the evening like he would do if only going for a run 
but waits until the next morning.
When he wakes up, he reduces his insulin to 85% 
and gets ready in his usual gear. While running he 
observes his blood glucose decreasing but is not 
worried as he will soon be reaching the training fa­
cilities. He starts strength training with a level of 4.5 
mmol/L which quickly increases to 7.2 mmol/L. 
When he gets home Rasmus looks at his data in the 
app. His blood glucose fits perfectly as he predicted 
from his prior experience in the app. He feels really 
happy about his success and makes a note to keep to 
this strategy. 
It fits well with his value statement: ”I want to know 
as much as possible about my disease so that I can 
optimize my approach to managing it.” (Persona 
comment in Figure 133).
Trying to go to bed
Louise does not dare to exercise like she used to be­
fore she got T1D. Each time she has been physically 
active, the entire next day has been ruined as she 
cannot get her blood glucose under control again be­
fore bedtime.
One day, Louise comes across ExerciseExpert whi­
le scrolling through Instagram and decides to try it 
out. She starts at level 1 and is surprised by the re­
latively simple guidelines which she cannot remem­
ber having heard about before. She receives her first 
“It is genius to collect my numbers in one place 
to get an overview! It is super simple but so 
effective.”
Figure 133: Rasmus’ reaction based on value statement
==PAGE:135==
Figure 134: Balanced life as new with type 1 diabetes
“It is really nice to get a personal tool to get better 
at exercising at my own pace! I would not have da­
red to start exercising again any time soon without 
this!”
challenge and feels reassured that she is aware of 
the important aspects of the short activity. After the 
challenge she feels good and thinks to herself that it 
was not too bad.
Two hours after the challenge ended, she receives a 
notification and compares the blood glucose levels. 
As she learned in the theory, she observes that it is 
still gradually decreasing and knows that she can 
take a bit less insulin for her next meal.
The next day she feels rested and well in her body 
and is excited to start the next level and try out a 
longer challenge. 
It fits well with her value statement: ”It is overwhel­
ming to learn so many new things. It is a struggle to 
make sure that I remember everything. I wish it was 
easier.” The persona comment is in Figure 135.
“It is super motivating to have my small victories 
celebrated! And as it gives me a better overview of 
my patterns especially regarding stress, I feel less 
worried. After only a couple of weeks, I had to sha­
re it with my followers on Instagram.” 
“It is nice that I can be part of a diabetes commu­
nity without risking my friends seeing it.
- But I would really not like to write down things 
before and after every day activities with friends 
just because I have diabetes.”

17 Justification
136
==PAGE:137==
17.1 Proposed Gold Solution
For the proposed gold solution, the concept is an in­
dividual mobile app, though it should ideally conne­
ct to other apps and receive data to allow the user to 
pull data from health- or diabetes apps.
Essential elements of the gold solution are seen in 
the Business Model Canvas (Figure 139, Works­
heet 31) and elaborated on in the following text 
(Osterwalder, A. & Pigneur, Y, 2021).
17.1.1 Value Proposition
The Value Proposition is to help the user un­
derstand how physical activity and influences 
on the body affects the blood glucose level.
This is obtained through the proposed learning 
structure where theory is combined with practical 
learning as well as the unique overview of data re­
garding exercise, blood glucose, and other factors. 
Furthermore, the Sharing function ensures inspirati­
on and help from others while also ensuring a com­
munity feeling.
17.1.2 Customer Segment
The concept is specifically targeted the 18-40-year-
olds who are the generation of Millennials (1981-
1996) and Generation Z (1997-2015). These are 
both tech-savvy and actively engaged in social me­
dia. The strategic design choices fit these generati­
ons as explained in the following.
17.1.2.1 Gamification- and Dialogue Elements
“Millennials prefer media channels which provide 
an option for dialogue or another way of giving them 
the possibility to give their opinion. But engagement 
can also be created with the help of gamification.” 
(Westmark-Højelsen, U.) 
Millennials would be engaged by the level structure 
and rewards, which creates gamification of the app. 
The Sharing function also engages them as a means 
to create dialogue and offer their opinion. 
17.1.2.3 Different Learning Style
As seen in Figure 138, the newer generation has a 
different perspective on learning. Lifelong learning 
and personal learning, e.g. continuously getting bet­
ter at managing their health, is valued. Feedback 
and instant gratification, which can be triggered by 
gamification elements as badges, is also a want for 
millennials (Westmark-Højelsen, U.). A characteri­
stic is also to learn by exploring which is done in the 
app with nudging to self-observe.
17.1.2.4 Size of the Target
Hedia, a Danish app for persons with T1D, which is 
currently expanding to the UK has more than 10.000 
downloads in Google Play. Data on the number of 
people in Denmark with Type 1 Diabetes is found 
from the RUKS (The register for chosen chronic il­
lnesses). It is not possible to choose specific ages, 
only age intervals which show: 
•	 6875 persons with T1D who are 20-39 years old.
•	 8525 persons with T1D who are 15-39 years old.
•	 10,500 persons with T1D in the age group 15-44 
years old. 
17.1.2.5 Scalability
As an app, the concept can easily be scaled globally 
137
as it is accessible in a virtual shop. It is also possible 
that adults over the age of 40 can benefit from it as 
similar T1D needs apply for them. Design elements 
for Millennials and Generation Z can also be useful 
for future generations. Furthermore, there are many 
more persons with T2D. Those who need insulin tre­
atment can also make use of the concept.
Going Global
T1D is a global disease. For instance, 1.6 million 
Americans are living with T1D. Global users will 
find the app in App Store or Google Play and is the­
reby reached with few resources. For turning the 
concept into an interesting business case, it is essen­
tial to look beyond national borders. What is needed 
is language translation, marketing outside of Den­
Figure 138: Infographic by Shift Disruptive E-learning
==PAGE:138==
Figure 139: Business Model for the Gold Solution
138
Gold Solution
==PAGE:139==
mark, and higher requirements for CE certification. 
Future Generations
The next generations fit into the same strategic 
choices in regard to the target group. The generation 
following Generation Z, Generation Alpha, is also 
called Generation Glass as the smartphone and iPad 
glass is a part of their every day from an early age. 
Thereby the concept is predicted to also fit this ge­
neration. Louise Byg Kongsholm, CEO of a scandi­
navian trend institute, Pej Gruppen, states (Rahbek, 
B., 2016):
“The companies in the forefront who want to be pre­
pared for the future are learning about the alphas. 
(...) Technology will be a fundamental dimension for 
play, learning and development.“
Adults with T1D Over the Age of 40
Most user research has been with adults up to 40 
years old and there has been made strategic choices 
based on this age group. However, persons with T1D 
older than 40 years have similar struggles of balan­
cing their blood glucose to factors besides food such 
as exercise, mood and hormones. These can thereby 
also become users of the concept, though depending 
on their level of technological advancement.
Persons with Other Diabetes Types
The app can be relevant for other diabetics who de­
pends on insulin. Up to 50 % of persons with T2D 
have a need for insulin treatment (Henriksen, J.E., 
2020). There are a total of 241,425 persons with 
T2D in Denmark whereas 16.705 are 20-44 years 
old. The prevalence is larger amongst the older age 
groups. The prevalence of T2D amongst Generation 
Z and Millenials is therefore expected to increase (as 
they get older).
17.1.3 Customer Relationships
The customer relationships should be close and 
users should be involved in  continuously improving 
the app to ensure that it fits the needs. The value pro­
position also relies on elements of a co-creation re­
lationship where the users are crucial to delivering a 
valuable Sharing function with content that is help­
ful to others (Osterwalder, A. & Pigneur, Y, 2021). 
Furthermore, gamification elements keep users en­
gaged as well as the unique overview of data. The 
close relationship is also ensured through personal 
assistance in the post-purchase support.
17.1.4 Channels
Awareness about the app is created by the use of the 
channels of The Danish Diabetes Association and 
through marketing on social media such as ads and 
collaborations with influencers on Instagram as this 
platform fits the customer segment. There are sever­
al influencers sharing their everyday with T1D, who 
are reaching a big audience of others with T1D and 
who thereby could be potential ambassadors.
The app should be available through  the indirect 
sales channels, App Store and Google Play.
Post-purchase support is provided personally by 
supporters which can be reached on the phone or 
email. As the value proposition has to do with as­
sisting in personal management of T1D the personal 
support is important for making the users feel cared 
for and understood.
139
17.1.5 Key Resources and -Activities
The key resources are mainly intellectual and hu­
man. Most importantly are the developers of the app 
such as programmers and user experience specia­
lists, who will perform the key activities of devel­
oping, maintaining, and continuously researching 
the needs of users as well as keep in contact with 
diabetes professionals. Other key activities are mar­
keting and support which is also done by human re­
sources. 
Data from other apps about activity, blood glucose, 
carb intake, etc. is also a highly important resource, 
which is needed for delivering the proposed value 
proposition. Acquiring this resource requires activi­
ties such as continuous research of the field as well 
as alignment and collaboration with key partners.
17.1.6 Key Partners
Data about activity, blood glucose, carb intake, etc. 
is a key resource which needs to be acquired from 
partners.The app will not directly compete with any 
of the key partners as it cannot replace the value 
they provide to the end users. Therefore the type of 
partnership would ideally be strategic alliances bet­
ween non-competitors where the partnership would 
benefit both companies. Alternatively it could also 
be buyer-supplier relationships where the app recei­
ves the data at an agreed price. Here it is still impor­
tant to ensure reliable access.
It is assumed that The Danish Diabetes Association 
would not need monetary incentive as part of the 
partnership. Furthermore, this partnership is also an 
important channel for reaching the customer seg­
ment.
==PAGE:140==
140
17.1.7 Cost Structure
The business model is characterized by having rela­
tively fixed costs. The biggest cost incurred is related 
to the activities and resources of developing the app. 
After launching the app, this cost is replaced with 
maintenance and continuous improvements. The 
app itself without connectivity is relatively simple 
and it does not require much maintenance of content 
as it relies on general guidelines and the user’s own 
self-learning.
As a unique app, the concept will be more costly than 
if developed as a web-app, which would, however, 
limit integration with the phone system and push no­
tifications which are important for the concept. This 
is also the case with modular apps (Wordpress etc.), 
which is also cheaper but very compromised.
A rough cost estimate for the unique app was given 
by Jeppe Chabert, Senior Business developer at Ap­
pWork.dk. He stated that the gamification elements 
are especially difficult to estimate but that the app 
can quickly run up to 100,000 DKK. However, as 
such estimates are difficult to give and can vary a 
lot, a conservative estimate is set at 250,000 DKK 
if hiring freelance programmers or consultants. 
This estimate is based on conversations with Johan 
Pedersen (IT Project Manager at Uptime Develop­
ments A/S) and Dan Roland Persson (PhD student 
at Cachet). Cachet (Copenhagen Center for Health 
Technology) has an open-source programming fra­
mework that can be used to bring down the costs 
(carp.cachet.dk). Integration of Google Fit is inclu­
ded and can be used without partnership with Go­
ogle. 
Another cost is marketing outside of The Danish 
Diabetes Association which is suggested to be th­
rough ads and collaborations on Instagram. 
The personal support setup is more expensive than 
other options such as support bots and is a conside­
rable part of the cost structure.
A big uncertainty regarding the cost structure is in 
relation to the collaborations with app companies 
from which the app should receive data. The colla­
boration should be mutually beneficial and it is the­
refore assumed that they would want some type of 
payment or exposure.
17.1.8 Revenue Streams
Ideally, the majority of the revenue streams would 
come from funding. Funding will play an important 
role in the development phase to ensure the needed 
funds for programming the app. Funding would also 
be ideal in order to be able to offer the app for free 
and thereby not create a monetary barrier for new 
users. However, different strategies have also been 
created for how the app could be offered for free 
without relying on funding. 
The design team was in contact with Martin Vester­
by, Health Tech Adoption Manager at Health Tech 
Innovation Hub Copenhagen (Appendix 15). He 
also emphasized that endocrinology is one of the 
areas which are receiving the most funding in Den­
mark. Steno Diabetes Center is pushing for it and 
Novo Nordisk is a C20 corporation who also drives 
innovation and research. The barrier to cover devel­
opment cost within this industry is therefore low. 
For revenue in the long-term, the app can potentially 
form a database of anonymized diabetes data or of 
diabetics willing to participate in research projects. 
Access to this database could then be sold to resear­
chers, companies for clinical trials, e.g. Studies&­
Me, or industry innovators such as Novo Nordisk. 
This strategy was recommended by Studies&Me as 
patient databases are much needed but expensive to 
buy access to.
Another option is to use a freemium pricing strategy 
(Dolgui, A., & Proth, J. M., 2010) with a subscripti­
on model. This entails revenue from ads in the free 
version and/or revenue from user payment for get­
ting a premium version. For comparison, MySugr 
has a Pro-version which costs 25 DKK per month.
==PAGE:141==
17.2 Implementation Plan for 
the Gold Solution
Important aspects of the implementation plan, besi­
des the development of the app, are testing in con­
text to measure the effect of using the concept and 
considerations regarding where the implementati­
on should take place. One of the best practices for 
implementation of apps is the LEAN methodology, 
where continuous testing is performed with an MVP. 
Therefore the implementation has been divided into 
a first- and second phase to not implement all ele­
ments at once. The implementation plan for the first 
phase of implementation can be seen in Figure 140.
17.2.1 Testing and Development
The concept should continuously be tested with 
users. Since the concept is based on continuous le­
arning, user testing in context would need to be con­
ducted for several weeks. In the initial stage, there 
would be a need for low fidelity prototyping to save 
time and programming costs. A simple paper mock-
up could be used to replicate the content of the app. 
For the elements which require interaction, e.g. no­
tifications to remember to measure blood glucose, 
other means such as text messages can be used.
Relevant questions to uncover when testing in con­
text are listed in Figure 141.
Once these questions have been answered at a satis­
factory level, the app should be developed and used 
for further testing.
17.2.2 Defining the MVP and Elements 
for Later Development
The sharing functionality is not needed in the MVP 
as it is not necessary for the primary flow of the app 
It can therefore be added after it has been launched 
to market. Defining favorite types of exercise forms 
is also not a need to have. It can be added later on to 
add more personalization.
17.2.3 Responsibles of Implementation
Steno Diabetes Center was contacted as it could 
be a place for implementation. According to their 
website, “The objective of Steno Diabetes Center 
Copenhagen (SDCC) is to offer world-class treat­
ment and contribute to better lives and equality in 
health for people with diabetes, their relatives and 
141
Figure 140: Implementation Plan
citizens at risk.” A meeting has therefore been set 
up so knowledge exchange beneficial for them can 
be conducted in the form of a concept presentation. 
The meeting will be on June 28th 2021 (three days 
after hand-in of the Master Thesis). Their interest 
and competencies could be used for a study setup 
which can make the concept strong in regards to re­
gulations where concrete Key Performance Indica­
tors (KPIs) can be measured as marked for further 
work in the Product idea model (Figure 142). A KPI 
could be the percentage increase in test score before 
and after use.
It can be offered as a student project, e.g., through 
the innovation hubs, DTU Skylab or KU SundHub, 
for students interested in areas within software, 
medico, user studies and entrepreneurship. This is 
where The Danish Diabetes Association functions 
as a touchpoint. They are in the process of making a 
website page on doing Bachelor- and Master theses 
in collaboration with them. The draft for the web­
site page is in Appendix 18 and made by the stu­
dent contact person, Anne-Marie Wegener. Wegener 
has also offered a project for 42090 Holistic Design 
of Engineering Systems which takes place in DTU 
Skylab and wants to get even more involved with 
student projects. Thereby contact with DTU Skylab

17.3 Changes in the Develop­
ment Arena
The concept will reshape the described development 
arena (Section 4.1) through network displacements 
of existing actors. A possible development arena af­
ter implementation of ExerciseExpert is sketched in 
Figure 134 (Worksheet 32).
Steno Diabetes Center, who is considered a possi­
ble owner of the concept, would play an important 
role in implementation as part of two central actor 
worlds. The app should be implemented as part of 
research and thereby feed into the Danish Knowled­
ge Centers. Steno Diabetes Center is in contact with 
persons with T1D at diagnosis and consultations as 
part of the actor world Treatment team. The concept 
is not seen as a treatment app and will not repla­
ce or displace any actors in treatment. However, it 
will be introduced to the market here and aid the 
patients in sustaining a healthy and active life with 
its continuous learning structure and linking of data. 
Thus a network displacement is created as Steno 
Diabetes Center will enter the actor world Diabe­
tes Tracking. Where they before have recommended 
diabetes tracking apps to patients, they will now of­
fer their own though not in direct competition with 
the existing offerings.
The concept in its full form is juxtaposed with 
existing tracking apps, as full functionality cannot 
be obtained without these which will form a techno­
logical ensemble of tracking apps.
The concept creates a network displacement of So­
cial networks for T1D. The online networks for T1D 
are currently controlled by social media giants (Fa­
cebook and Instagram) and administered by other 
persons with T1D. There is no distinction between 
who is posting. With the concept, the Danish health 
care system will take control of a part of the ongoing 
information sharing and advice from others will be 
filtered by level and interest. Hereby creating a new 
channel for information to the flow model presented 
in the Exploration phase (section 3.3.4). 
When developing the concept there are potential 
143
conflicts in the development arena to be aware of. 
Though it is the strategy of the concept not to give 
any treatment advice to avoid being categorized as 
a medical device there is a risk that it will still need 
a degree of CE certification for medical equipment. 
Furthermore, the conflict regarding the access to 
glucose sensors and test strips for finger pricking in 
the Municipalities can affect the use of the concept 
for users who do not have the option to measure 
blood glucose as frequently as others.

17.4 Silver Solution
The silver solution is a version of the concept which 
is assessed as being easier to implement, though not 
necessarily at a lower cost. 
In the silver solution, it is proposed to integrate the 
concept in an existing app. Integrating the concept in 
an existing app saves time in implementation as the 
platform will already be developed though it needs 
to be expanded. For a silver solution, the concept 
should be kept as its core in order to fit the vision of 
the current app to integrate with.
17.4.1 Changes to Business Model
There are changes compared to the gold solution 
in the Business Model Canvas as marked in Figu­
re 145. Elements from the gold solution which does 
not apply to the silver solution are crossed out. 
In the silver solution, it would be the other app’s 
existing channels, key partners, cost structure, and 
revenue stream. The customer segments would also 
be different depending on who the current users are 
of the existing app.
17.4.2 Apps for Integration
There are several apps which the concept could be 
integrated with. In this section, some of these al­
ternatives will be described to highlight how they 
would each influence the concept.
17.4.2.1 ’Diabetes og kulhydrattælling’
This carb counting app is owned by The Danish 
Diabetes Association and thereby there would not 
be a barrier of collaboration. However, there is no 
one working on the app anymore and the Danish 
Diabetes Association has started to recommend He­
dia instead (Figure 144).
17.4.2.2 Hedia
The Hedia app aids the user in getting a stable blood 
glucose level and through logging of blood glucose 
levels, carbs, exercise activities, and active insulin it 
calculates and recommends insulin doses. The holi­
stic approach to diabetes management means that the 
app looks after the general interests of the user. This 
is a benefit for a possible collaboration as it ensures 
better possibilities for connectivity. Additionally, 
the logging of data is beneficial for the concept as 
it can easily be integrated. Hedia is CE-marked as a 
medical device, which reduces the health regulatory 
limitations with regards to what the concept can do. 
However, integration comes with the risk of Exerci­
seExpert being hidden among many other features.
Feasibility of Integration with Hedia
To explore the opportunities for a possible collabo­
ration with Hedia, a meeting was held with Louise 
Berg Larsson. The meeting also served as a means 
to further demarcate Hedia’s area of operation and 
influence feeding into the understanding of the de­
velopment arena.
Hedia wants to maintain a clear focus on the insulin 
calculator as the main feature of the app. Therefo­
re it does not seem feasible to integrate the concept 
with Hedia as it would dilute the value proposition 
of Hedia. However, according to Larsson a market 
is emerging of module based apps, where the apps 
are fully integrated and link to each other but with 
their own separate value proposition. 
Figure 144: From the left: ’Diabetes og kulhydrattælling’, ’Hedia’ and ’mySugr’
==PAGE:145==
145
Figure 145: Business Model Canvas for the Silver Solution (Gold Solution items are crossed out)
Silver Solution
==PAGE:146==
146
Though a module based integration could add value 
for Hedia, they currently have a lot of work in the 
pipeline, keeping them busy with their core business 
at least a year forward. It would therefore be dif­
ficult for them to take in such a project.
17.4.2.3 mySugr
mySugr is an alternative to Hedia. mySugr already 
uses gamification elements such as challenges (Fi­
gure 146) and ExerciseExpert might therefore fit 
their approach better than Hedia. 
mySugr is a product from Roche Diabetes who has 
built an open ecosystem to ensure a connection bet­
ween their own and other brands for personal dia­
betes management. This approach fits well with the 
strategy of collecting data from other apps and use 
in ExerciseExpert. Furthermore, the aspect of inte­
grating with other brands ensures that the concept 
will not become brand specific.
In regards to revenue, mySugr has both a free and a 
pro version, which would also become the pricing 
strategy of ExerciseExpert if integrating with my­
Sugr. Roche Diabetes Care offers the pro version 
for free if the user has one of their devices which 
means the commercial aspect influences what ver­
sion a user can get. This can be problematic in the 
context of the Danish health care system, where 
users cannot decide themselves which brands to use.
17.4.2.4 Competitive Advantage of Integra­
ting with Carb Counting Apps
By introducing a learning game, carb counting apps 
can make competition irrelevant as they would of­
fer a unique value proposition. A strategy canvas 
was made to illustrate mySugr and Hedia’s place­
ment in the market with and without integration of 
the ExerciseExpert concept (Figure 137). The cur­
rent competitive advantage of Hedia is their holi­
stic approach where they, contrary to mySugr, also 
include a focus on exercise when calculating and 
recommending insulin doses. Apart from empha­
sizing their focus on exercise, the offering level of 
fun would also increase substantially, placing them 
more directly in competition with mySugr on this 
aspect. mySugr’s current competitive advantage is 
its gamification approach, making it a more fun app, 
which would be emphasized by the concept. mySugr 
Price
Fun
Self-
learning
Data-intelligent 
suggestions
Focus on Exercise
Offering Level
Low
High
Hedia
MySugr with EE
EE: Short for ExerciseExpert
MySugr
Hedia with EE
would especially benefit from the focus on exercise 
when competing with Hedia.
17.4.2.5 Continous Glucose Monitoring Apps 
Another alternative is to integrate the concept in an 
app made for one of the continuous glucose moni­
toring systems such as Freestyle LibreLink or Dex­
com G6, which are both common brands used in 
Denmark.
Both apps are quite simple with interfaces that focus 
on the current blood glucose level as well as data 
overview. Thereby the concept would not risk being 
hidden behind multiple features, however, it might 
not be favorable for the companies to add the con­
cept, which takes up a large amount of space com­
pared to the rest of the app. The continuous glucose 
monitoring apps could therefore risk making their 
simple interface complex by integrating the concept. 
If integrating with these sensor apps there is a risk 
of delimiting the customer segment to users of the 
specific brand, as the brand may not be interested 
in cooperating with competing sensor apps to let 
users draw data from those. This would also mean a 
change in key partners as it would not be relevant to 
partner up with other sensor companies. Like it was 
described for integration with mySugr, such implica­
tions can be problematic in the context of the Danish 
health care system, where users cannot decide them­
selves which brands to use.
Figure 146: Strategy Canvas
==PAGE:147==
147
17.4.2.6 Fitness Apps
It could be used as a module in exercise apps to 
expand their target group to include more persons 
with T1D. This could for instance be Fitbit. This 
could be a way to expand their business from a focus 
on exercise enthusiasts who focus on fitness perfor­
mance to also cover people who have a larger need 
to track their daily rhythms to avoid sickness (per­
sons with T1D).
Strengths and Weaknesses of the silver solution 
compared to the gold solution are seen in Table 32.
17.5 Bronze Solution
A bronze solution is included to present how the 
concept could unfold in a manner that is easy and 
quick to implement with few resources. The Busi­
ness Model Canvas in Figure 150 summarizes the 
key aspects of the bronze solution and indicates the 
changes compared to the gold- and silver solution. 
For the bronze solution, it is suggested to integra­
te the proposed learning structure on the website of 
The Danish Diabetes Association, thereby making 
them the owners.
Knowledge and guidelines about exercising with 
type 1 diabetes already exist on the website in the tab 
‘Health’-’Exercise’-’Exercise and type 1’,  which is 
also where the bronze solution would live (Figure 
147).
However, to ensure a motivating learning journey, 
the theory and challenges should be implemented as 
collapsed levels which can be expanded to only pre­
sent the users with small bites of knowledge.
Figure 147: Website Structure. Knowledge is currently 
placed under ‘Sundhed’ [Health], ‘Motion’ [Exercise] 
and ‘Motion og type 1’ [Exercise and type 1].
To create awareness and create more interactive 
learning, The Danish Diabetes Association should 
invite their Instagram followers to participate in a 
month of exercise theory and challenges. Using the 
Instagram Stories function, theory can be shared 
in small bites, quiz questions can be asked in the 
existing quiz function, and everyone can be instruc­
ted and encouraged to do the challenges and share 
their experiences (Figure 148).
The strengths and weaknesses of the bronze solution 
can be seen in Table 33.

for them to implement. Furthermore, they show­

create proof concept to create interest from potential 
partners of the gold solution as well a foundation for 
applying for funding for development.

17.6 Final Validation and Eval­
uation
To evaluate the concept (gold solution), potential 
users were invited for a usability test for feedback 
and scoring of the solution based on the system usa­
bility score (SUS). The design team evaluated its 
value and impact in the engineering system and eva­
luated it based on the task and goal.
17.6.1 User Validation
The user validation was conducted with nine poten­
tial users where five of these were the ones who had 
taken part in usability testing while the remaining 
four had only seen the very early concept during the 
concept synthesis phase. This was done to ensure 
that the validation would not become biased which 
could be a risk if only involving the five who had 
been closely involved in the detailing. 
The potential users were given the final prototype 
and a think-aloud test was conducted. After the test, 
they were presented with the ten statements of the 
system usability score and asked to rate them bet­
ween 1 (strongly disagree) and 5 (strongly agree) as 
seen in Figure 151. 
17.6.1.1 Overall Feedback
The app concept was generally well received also by 
the potential users who did not have it as a favorite 
during the Concept Synthesis phase. The feedback 
given was that the concept could do more and was 
more applicable for them than what they had imagi­
ned when it was initially presented. 
“It is way better than I had expected. I didn’t 
think I would be in the target group. I would 
definitely use it because I can see my patterns.” 
- Nicole, T1D, 20 y/o
A highlighted feature is that blood glucose is presen­
ted before and after exercise. 
“I especially like that I can see my blood sugar 
before and after because that is where it usu­
ally goes wrong because I don’t know when it 
went wrong.” - Signe J, T1D, 25 y/o
For Michael who rated the concept high in the Con­
cept Synthesis phase, presenting the blood glucose 
in relation to exercise habits was the highlight.
“It is the absolute best of these kinds of pro­
jects I have participated in. And it is not even 
technically advanced - it is just using what you 
already have.”- Michael, T1D, 38 y/o
Figure 151: System Usability Score. Picture from Gutiérrez-Carreón, G., et al. (2015). 
==PAGE:151==
151
Additionally, many of the participants showed great 
interest in the perspectives of development as they 
wanted to start using the concept as soon as possi­
ble.
17.6.1.2 System Usability Score
The system usability score (SUS) is calculated in 
the following way:
SUS = (X + Y) x 2.5
where,
X = Sum of the points for all odd-numbered ques­
tions – 5
Y = 25 – Sum of the points for all even-numbered 
questions
Below the average SUSs are presented respectively 
for the participants involved in usability testing and 
for those who were not. The score for each partici­
pant can be seen in Table 34. The answers are on 
Appendix 15.
Average for participants involved in usability 
testing:
87.5
Average for participants who had not seen any 
prototypes before:
88.5
The usability was acceptable as it was rated higher 
than the average SUS which is around 70. Further­
more, according to the scoring chart seen in Figure 
152, the app concept scored excellent based on the 
calculated averages. 
The statement which generally got the lowest score 
was “7. I imagine that most people would learn to 
use this system very quickly.” 8/9 participants sco­
red it lower than 5 though they all elaborated that 
their reasoning was that they thought it would be 
more difficult for the elderly to use. Therefore this is 
seen as acceptable as elderly are not included in the 
primary target group.
Furthermore, Thippe had a specific reflection upon 
the target group as she at this moment could not see 
herself using it because her daily activities vary gre­
atly. However, she said she could see herself using 
it a lot after the summer break, where she starts her 
carpenter apprenticeship and her day-to-day routi­
nes become more steady state.
17.6.1.3 Relevant Further Work
Besides the overall feedback and comments given 
in relation to the SUS, the participants had a few 
comments for how the concept could be improved:
•	 Consider changing from a hamburger menu to a 
more visible indication of menu items.
•	 Consider making the most important touch points 
more distinct to guide the user. E.g., the blue 
color of the buttons were not distinct enough to 
some, which was especially seen for the ‘Lear­
ning Journey’ button on the home page, as this 
screen is busy with other visual elements.
•	 More focus on correlation between blood glucose 
and exercise during the day instead of a whole 
week. Furthermore, color could be added to indi­
cate which days went badly with regards to the 
blood glucose level and/or color to indicate the 
activity intensity.
•	 The number of clicks could be limited, though it 
was noted that the simplicity and small amount of 
information on each page should be maintained.
•	 It should be considered how the Sharing function 
can be moderated to avoid unpleasant comments 
when it is implemented.

17.6.2 Evaluation and Impact Across En­
gineering System Levels
Apart from validating with users, the concept is eva­
luated in accordance with the set design task, goal 
formulation and its value and impact on the three 
system engineering levels.
The design task was formulated as:
The design team will create concepts that im­
prove the life of type 1 diabetics by increasing 
predictability in daily life while taking individu­
ality and a variety of physiological states into 
account. The concepts will aid the person with 
type 1 diabetes in managing their disease and 
function as a boundary object which benefits 
the collaboration between the person and their 
surroundings.
The concept, ExerciseExpert, is developed as an 
app-artefact. It is a digital solution, which improves 
the life of type 1 diabetics through a theoretical and 
practical learning journey where the predictability 
of blood glucose levels in relation to exercising is 
gradually increased. The learning structure is built 
around individuality with personal observations and 
room for taking various physical- and mental aspe­
cts into account.
It both translates knowledges and phenomena to and 
from the end user. It functions as a boundary object 
since it provides guidelines from The Danish Dia­
betes Association and research papers to the user. 
Tracking of exercise in relation to blood glucose 
also gives the end user a starting point to describe 
their experience and struggles to others, e.g. friends 
or medical professionals. 
As a digital artefact, it fits well into the context of 
type 1 diabetics, who comprise the Complex System 
Level together with The Danish Diabetes Associati­
on. In alignment with the findings of the Explorati­
on phase, people with T1D need solutions which are 
not a pain to bring with them in daily life like phy­
sical products can be. Additionally, a large part of 
persons with T1D, especially those within the target 
group of 18-40-year-olds, are used to using various 
apps for managing the condition.
The concept impacts the Complex System Level po­
sitively as validated with users. Furthermore, Exer­
ciseExpert is expected to also have a positive impact 
on the Engineering System Level, as it both moti­
vates its users to sustain a more active and healthy 
lifestyle and aids in learning how to maintain a sta­
ble blood glucose level. These aspects are general­
ly associated with a lower insulin need as well as a 
lower risk of developing comorbidities, thus crea­
ting value to the Danish Society.
On a larger, societal Engineering 
System level, the concept goes 
well in line with SDG 3, target 
3.d: “Strengthen the capacity 
of all countries, in particular 
developing countries, for early 
warning, risk reduction and ma­
nagement of national and global 
health risks”.
==PAGE:153==
153
Figure 153: Value Framework
17.6.2.1 Value Creation
The value of the concept shown in the Value Fra­
mework (Den Ouden, E., 2012) is in the psychology- 
and sociology- aspect (Figure 153). These are closely 
connected and aligned between the user, organisati­
on (The Danish Diabetes Association), ecosystem, 
and society. There is a shared drive for promoting a 
healthy lifestyle and it goes in line with the Danish 
Diabetes Association’s mission statement of impro­
ving the living conditions for persons with diabe­
tes. From a sociological standpoint, it goes well in 
line with the placement of Denmark on the Hofstede 
scale. For the cultural dimension of individualism, 
Denmark has a score of 74 out of 100 (Hofstede In­
sights). Thereby it is valuable to have a tool to first 
and foremost look after one’s own individual needs. 
Denmark has a score of 16 out of 100 in masculinity. 
According to Hofstede this means that relations, qu­
ality of life and room for failing are prioritized. This 
goes in line with the premise of being an Exercise­
Expert is not in being the best in a group in a sport or 
keeping their blood glucose level stable. Becoming 
an ExerciseExpert is by giving oneself the most time 
to develop self-awareness regarding exercise. Suc­
cessful use of the concept can result in economic 
value but it is far-fetched to state this early in the 
conceptual phase. Tests in context are still needed in 
the conceptual work in order to provide insights on 
the health results of using the app.

The easier it is to talk about T1D, the 
better.
Limited exposure to the general public. 
Table 35: Goal specification fulfillment for the Engineering System Level
154
17.6.2.2 Fulfilling the Goal Specification
The primary goal was to: 
Increase predictability in the daily life of young 
adults who have been diagnosed with T1D and 
are accustomed to the routines of the disease. 
The concept increases predictability by giving the 
users knowledge on which factors to consider and 
helps the user to track activities to better predict 
future activities. It accommodates users who are 
accustomed to the routines of the disease, as it gi­
ves a more detailed way of tracking than solely carb 
counting.
The concept is assessed in relation to the require­
ments and criteria listed in the goal specification. 
For each requirement and criteria it is elaborated 
how ExerciseExpert (in its gold solution form) ac­
commodates them in Tables 35, 36 and 37.
As the requirements were formulated at a stage whe­
re the solution space was still quite broad, some do 
not apply to the final solution and are therefore not 
elaborated.
